Ruling When Master Frees His Mukatab
States the ruling and the underlying qiyas-based proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master frees his mukatab slave and explain why the kitabah payment drops using the principle of consideration (badal).

Core Idea
The kitabah payment drops automatically when the slave is freed because the payment was in exchange for freedom.

This lesson examines a clear ruling from the Hidayah: when a master voluntarily frees his mukatab (a slave who has contracted for freedom by paying installments), the slave becomes free immediately and the remaining kitabah debt is forgiven. The reasoning rests on a fundamental contractual principle—the consideration (badal) is only due when the countervalue (freedom) is obtained through payment. Since freedom is granted directly, the obligation to pay ceases. We also explore why the slave's consent to dissolve the contract is presumed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the specific action discussed (master freeing his mukatab) and the legal outcome: the slave becomes free and the kitabah payment is dropped.
This is the core ruling—the rest of the passage justifies it.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Find the evidence cited for this ruling. Here it is a qiyas (analogy): the kitabah payment is like a price in a sale; if the subject matter is destroyed, the price is not owed.
The proof is aqli (rational) rather than naqli (textual).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (indication of the proof)
Understand how the analogy works: the slave undertook payment solely to obtain freedom. Since freedom is achieved without payment, the obligation to pay is automatically voided.
The key link is that the payment is causally tied to freedom as its consideration.
4
Note the role of the slave's consent
Although the kitabah contract is binding on the master (he cannot unilaterally cancel it), it can be dissolved with the slave's consent. The text argues that the slave's consent is evident because he gains freedom and retains his earnings.
This prevents the master from unilaterally dissolving the contract, but freeing is a separate act that triggers the debt drop.
5
Determine the practical outcome
Summarize the consequences: the slave is freed, owes nothing, and keeps any earnings he made during the kitabah period (since the kitabah was kept in effect for his benefit).
This ruling is straightforward and accepted across schools (muttafaq alayh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال . واذا اعتق المولى مكاتبه عتق باعتاقه لقيام ملكه فيه وسقط بدل الكتابة لانه ما التزمه الا مقابلا بالعتق وقد حصل له دونه فلا يلزمه والكتابة وان كانت لازمة فى جانب المولى ولكنها تفسخ برضاء العبد والظاهر رضاه توسلا الى عتقه بغير بدل مع سلامة الاكساب له لانا نبقى الكتابة في حقه
جب آقا نے اپنی مکاتبہ کو آزاد کیا تو وہ اس کے آزاد کرنے سے آزاد ہو جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ اس میں آقا کی ملک قائم ہے اور بدل کتابت ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے کہ اس نے مال کا التزام نہیں کیا تھا مگر آزادی کے مقابلہ میں اور وہ اس کے بغیر ہی حاصل ہو گئی۔ لہنہ امال لازم نہ ہوگا۔ اور کتابت اگر چہ آقا کی جانب میں لازم ہوتی ہے۔ لیکن غلام کی رضامندی سے فتح ہو جاتی ہے اور ظاہر اس کی رضا مندی ہی ہے بلا مال اپنی آزادی کا وسیلہ کرنے کے لئے اپنی کمائی سالم ومحفوظ رہنے کے ساتھ کیونکہ کمائی کے حق میں ہم نے اس کی کتابت کو باقی رکھا ہے۔
قوله واذا اعتق المولى ... الخ - اگر آقا نے اپنی مکاتبہ کو آزاد کر دیا تو وہ اس کے آزاد کرنے سے آزاد ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ اس میں آقا کی ملک قائم ہے اس لئے کہ پہلے یہ بات معلوم ہو چکی کہ مکاتب پر جب تک ایک درہم بھی باقی رہے وہ غلام ہی رہتا ہے اور جب وہ آزاد ہو گیا تو بدل کتابت ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے کہ مال کا التزام تو اس نے اس لئے کیا تھا کہ اس کے مقابلہ میں آزادی حاصل ہو اور یہاں وہ بلا مال آزاد ہو گیا۔ اور عقد کتابت اگر چہ آقا کی جانب میں لازم ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی وہ اس کو تو ڑ نہیں سکتا لیکن غلام کی رضامندی سے فتح ہو جاتا ہے اور یہ ظاہر ہے کہ جب غلام کو اس کی کمائی مل رہی ہے۔ بایں معنی کہ کمائی کے حق میں ہم نے اس کی کتابت کو باقی رکھا ہے اور اس کو آزادی بھی مفت حاصل ہو رہی ہے تو وہ ضرور اس بات پر راضی ہوگا کہ عقد کتابت ٹوٹ جائے۔
He said: And if the master frees his mukatab, he becomes free by virtue of his emancipation because the master's ownership still exists over him, and the kitabah payment is dropped because he (the slave) did not undertake it except in exchange for freedom, and it (freedom) has been obtained without it, so it does not become binding upon him. And although the kitabah is binding on the side of the master, it is voided by the slave's consent, and apparently his consent is given as a means to his freedom without payment along with the safety of his earnings for him, because we keep the kitabah in effect with respect to his earnings. When the master freed his mukatabah, she will be free by his freeing because the master's ownership is established over her. The kitabah payment will drop, because he had not undertaken the payment except in exchange for freedom, and it has been obtained without it, so it will not be binding. And even though the kitabah is binding on the side of the master, it is dissolved with the consent of the slave, and his consent is evident: to obtain his freedom without payment while his earnings remain safe and sound, because we keep the kitabah in effect with respect to his earnings. He said: 'And if the master frees his mukatab...' – If the master freed his mukatabah, she will be free by his freeing because the master's ownership is established over her. It has been previously established that as long as even one dirham remains unpaid, the mukatab remains a slave. When he becomes free, the kitabah payment will drop because he undertook the payment in exchange for freedom, and here he has become free without payment. The kitabah contract, even though binding on the master, is dissolved by the slave's consent. It is evident that when the slave is getting his earnings, meaning we have kept his kitabah intact for the sake of his earnings, and he is also getting freedom for free, he will surely consent to the dissolution of the kitabah contract.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hidayah explaining the consequence when a master voluntarily frees his mukatab slave. The ruling is clear: the slave becomes free immediately by the master's act, and the kitabah payment is automatically dropped. The reasoning is that the kitabah payment was only undertaken by the slave in exchange for freedom. Since freedom has been granted without the payment, the obligation to pay ceases to exist. Although the kitabah contract is binding on the master (i.e., he cannot unilaterally cancel it), it can be dissolved with the consent of the slave. The text argues that the slave's consent is presumed because he gains freedom without payment and also retains his earnings (since the kitabah is kept in effect for the purposes of his earnings, implying that the slave's earnings during the kitabah period are his own after freedom). This ruling is based on the principle that the consideration (badal) in the kitabah is specifically for the emancipation, and once emancipation occurs through another means, the consideration falls away. The commentary reinforces that the master's ownership over the slave remains until full payment, but freeing the slave prior to completion is an act of virtue that extinguishes the debt.

Key Terms
بدل کتابت (badal al-kitabah): The payment that the mukatab slave owes to the master under the kitabah contract.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment or dissolution of a contract; here the kitabah is dissolved by the slave's consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a master frees his mukatab slave before the kitabah payment is completed, what happens to the kitabah debt?
A) The debt remains and must be paid by the freed slave.
B) The debt is dropped because the payment was in exchange for freedom which is already achieved.
C) The debt is transferred to the master's heirs.
D) The slave must pay half of the remaining debt.

Key Takeaway
The kitabah debt drops automatically when the master frees the mukatab because the payment was consideration for freedom, and the slave's consent is presumed due to the obvious benefit.

Tadbir of the Mukatabah and Her Options
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tadbir-mukatabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling when a master makes his mukatabah mudabbara, identify the options available to the slave, and state the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the amount payable upon her death.

Core Idea
A mukatabah made mudabbara has two paths to freedom; how much she pays is disputed among Hanafi imams.

This passage from Al-Hidayah deals with a slave-girl who has a kitabah contract and is then declared mudabbara by her master. The author explains that the tadbir is valid because she now has two potential paths to freedom. She may choose to complete the kitabah or abandon it and become a regular mudabbara. The passage then explores a specific scenario: if she dies before completing the kitabah, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises over whether she (or her estate) has a choice in the amount to be paid or must pay the lesser amount. Understanding this dispute hinges on whether the kitabah contract is considered binding on the slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the master making his mukatabah mudabbara. The ruling is that this tadbir is valid because the slave-girl 'receives two directions of freedom'.
2
State the options available to the mudabbara mukatabah
She can either complete the kitabah contract or declare herself incapable (ta'jiz) and become a regular mudabbara who will be free upon the master's death. This follows from the Hanafi principle that the kitabah is not binding on the slave.
3
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute on the amount payable upon her death
If she dies before completing the kitabah, both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree the amount is two-thirds (since the tadbir covers one-third of her value). The dispute is whether she has a choice to pay two-thirds of the kitabah price or two-thirds of her value (Abu Hanifa) or must pay the lesser of the two (Sahibayn). The underlying basis is whether the kitabah contract is considered binding on the slave.
4
Note the later scholarly difference on the slave's authority to end the kitabah
Muhammad ibn Salama held that the master's objection could prevent the slave from declaring incapacity, while Abu Bakr al-Balkhi stated the classical view that the slave has full authority. This is a secondary dispute among later mashayikh.
This step is tangential but present in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح قوله وان دبر مكاتبته ... الخ - اگر آقا نے اپنی مکاتبہ باندی کومد برہ کیا تو بدلیل مذکور (انه تلقتها جهتا حریة ) مد بر کرنا صحیح ہے۔ اب اس مکاتبہ مدبرہ کو اختیار ہے چاہے عقد کتابت پورا کر لے چاہے خود کو عاجز کر کے مدبرہ ہو جائے ۔ اس لئے کہ احناف وائمہ ثلاثہ کے نزدیک عقد کتابت مملوک کی جانب میں لازمی نہیں ہوتا ۔ پھر ذخیرہ میں ہے کہ اس مسئلہ کی بابت مشائخ کا اختلاف ہے کہ جب مکاتب خود کو عاجز قرار دینا چاہے اور آقا اس کو نہ مانے تو اس سے کتابت فسخ ہو جائے گی یا نہیں؟ سومحمد بن سلمہ کہتے ہیں کہ آقا اگر اس کی تعجیز کا انکار کرے تو اس کو اس کا اختیار ہے۔ لیکن شیخ ابو بکر بنی فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ بات اس کے بالکل خلاف ہے جو ہمارے اصحاب نے اپنی کتابوں میں ذکر کیا ہے کہ اس میں غلام خود مختار ہے۔
قوله فان مضت ..... الخ - اگر مکاتب معتبرہ مذکورہ نے عقد کتابت، پورا کرنا اختیار کیا اور اس کے پورا ہونے سے پہلے آ قام گیا اور اس باندی کے علاوہ کچھ مال ہے نہیں تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک وہ مختار ہو گی چاہے دو تہائی مال کتابت میں سعایت کرے چاہے اپنی دو تہائی قیمت میں ۔ صاحبین کے نزدیک اختیار نہیں بلکہ صرف ان دونوں میں سے کمتر مقدار میں سعایت کرے گی۔ پس یہاں صرف مختار ہونے میں اختلاف ہے جو اعتناق کے متجری ہونے اور نہ ہونے پر مبنی ہے۔ رہی مقدار سو وہ سب کے نزدیک دو تہائی متعین ہے۔ کیونکہ عوض بمقابلہ کل رقبہ کے ہے تو استحقاق ثابت نہ ہوا اس لئے کہ مد برہ تو اس کے بعد ہوئی ہے۔
Commentary on the statement: 'And if he makes his mukatabah mudabbara...' - If the master made his slave-girl who is a mukatabah mudabbara, then due to the mentioned proof (that she is receiving two directions of freedom) the tadbir is valid. Now this mudabbara mukatabah has an option: either she completes the kitabah contract or she declares herself incapable and becomes mudabbara. Because according to the Hanafis and the Three Imams, the kitabah contract is not binding on the slave's side. Then in al-Dhakhira it is mentioned that there is a difference among the mashayikh regarding this issue: when the mukatab wishes to declare himself incapable and the master does not accept it, is the kitabah rescinded or not? Muhammad ibn Salama says that if the master denies his declaration of incapacity, he has the option. But Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Balkhi states that this is completely contrary to what our companions have mentioned in their books, that in this matter the slave has full authority. Statement: 'So if she passes away...' - If the aforementioned trusted mukatabah chose to complete the kitabah contract, and before its completion the master died, and she has no property besides herself, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa she has a choice: either she pays two-thirds of the kitabah price or two-thirds of her value. According to the Sahibayn, she has no choice; she must only pay whichever is less. So here the difference is only in having the choice, which is based on whether the contract is binding or not. As for the amount, it is two-thirds according to all, because the consideration is against the total slave value, so entitlement is not established since the tadbir occurred after.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage deals with the case of a master who declares his mukatabah slave-girl as mudabbara (i.e., she will become free upon his death). The author explains that this tadbir is valid because the slave-girl now has two potential paths to freedom: the kitabah and the tadbir. The mukatabah has the option to either continue with the kitabah contract or declare herself incapable (ta'jiz) and thereby revert to being a regular mudabbara who will be free at the master's death. This is because the Hanafis (and the three other imams) hold that the kitabah is not binding on the slave; the slave is not forced to remain in the contract. The text then mentions a dispute among later scholars: if the mukatab wants to declare incapacity but the master refuses, is the contract rescinded? Muhammad ibn Salama says the master's objection matters, but Abu Bakr al-Balkhi disagrees, stating that the classical Hanafi position is that the mukatab has full authority to end the contract regardless. Next, a specific scenario: if the mukatabah (who is both mukatabah and mudabbara) dies before completing the kitabah and leaves no property except herself, how is the consideration handled? Imam Abu Hanifa gives her a choice to pay either two-thirds of the kitabah price or two-thirds of her market value, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say no choice: she must pay the lesser of the two. The amount is fixed at two-thirds because the consideration is for the entire slave value, but since she was made mudabbara, only part of her value belongs to the master.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract (kitabah) with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who is declared by the master to become free upon the master's death (tadbir).
تعجیز (ta'jiz): The act of a mukatab declaring himself incapable of completing the kitabah payments, thereby rescinding the contract.
متجری (mutajari): A contract that is binding and cannot be unilaterally rescinded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), what obligation arises when a mudabbara mukatabah dies before completing her kitabah and leaves no property?
A) Her estate must pay the full kitabah price.
B) Her estate must pay two-thirds of the kitabah price, with a choice between that and two-thirds of her value.
C) Her estate must pay the lesser of two-thirds of the kitabah price and two-thirds of her value, with no choice.
D) No payment is due because the tadbir frees her completely.

Key Takeaway
A mukatabah who is made mudabbara can choose to continue or abandon her kitabah; if she dies before completion, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises over whether her estate has a choice in the payment amount, but the mufta bihi obliges the lesser amount without choice.

Master Emancipating His Mukatabah
Ruling that the mukatabah becomes free immediately upon emancipation by the master.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master emancipates his mukatabah and explain the legal reasoning for the waiver of the kitabah payment.

Core Idea
The master's unilateral emancipation frees the mukatabah immediately and cancels the debt.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in the Kitabah contract: what happens when the master directly frees his mukatabah slave-girl. The Hanafi position is clear that the mukatabah becomes free instantly and the remaining badal kitabah is waived. This ruling is based on the master's continuing ownership and the slave's presumed consent. We will unpack the legal reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is the master setting free his female mukatabah. The ruling is that she becomes free immediately and the kitabah payment is dropped.
2
Locate the Dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) based on the master's ownership. Since the master still owns the slave until full payment, his emancipation is valid.
The dalil is rational (aqli) not textual.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The master's full ownership allows him to emancipate at any time. Once freed, the purpose of the kitabah contract (obtaining freedom) is fulfilled, so the debt falls away.
The slave's consent is presumed because she benefits.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Other schools may require the slave's formal acceptance or consider the contract still binding after emancipation.
Hanafi position is unique in full indemnity.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The Hanafi position is consistent with the principle that kitabah does not negate the master's ownership.
No intra-Hanafi dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله واذا اعتق المولى ... الخ - اگر آقا نے اپنی مکاتبہ کو آزاد کر دیا تو وہ اس کے آزاد کرنے سے آزاد ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ اس میں آقا کی ملک قائم ہے اس لئے کہ پہلے یہ بات معلوم ہو چکی کہ مکاتب پر جب تک ایک درہم بھی باقی رہے وہ غلام ہی رہتا ہے اور جب وہ آزاد ہو گیا تو بدل کتابت ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے کہ مال کا التزام تو اس نے اس لئے کیا تھا کہ اس کے مقابلہ میں آزادی حاصل ہو اور یہاں وہ بلا مال آزاد ہو گیا۔ اور عقد کتابت اگر چہ آقا کی جانب میں لازم ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی وہ اس کو تو ڑ نہیں سکتا لیکن غلام کی رضامندی سے فتح ہو جاتا ہے اور یہ ظاہر ہے کہ جب غلام کو اس کی کمائی مل رہی ہے۔ بایں معنی کہ کمائی کے حق میں ہم نے اس کی کتابت کو باقی رکھا ہے اور اس کو آزادی بھی مفت حاصل ہو رہی ہے تو وہ ضرور اس بات پر راضی ہوگا کہ عقد کتابت ٹوٹ جائے۔
Statement: 'And if the master emancipates...' - If the master set his female mukatabah free, then she becomes free by his freeing. Because in her the master's ownership is established, since it is already known that the mukatab remains a slave so long as even one dirham remains (unpaid). And when she is freed, the kitabah payment falls off. Because she undertook the payment only to obtain freedom in exchange, and here she receives freedom without payment. And the kitabah contract is binding on the master's side, meaning he cannot break it, but it dissolves with the slave's consent. And it is obvious that when the slave is receiving her earnings (meaning we have kept her kitabah intact for the right of earnings), and she also receives freedom for free, she will certainly be pleased that the kitabah contract is broken.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that when a master directly emancipates his mukatabah slave-girl, she becomes free immediately by his act of emancipation. The reason is that the master still owns the slave, and slavery persists until the full kitabah payment is made. Since the emancipation supersedes the contract, the remaining unpaid amount (badal kitabah) is dropped. The kitabah contract is binding on the master (he cannot cancel it unilaterally), but it can be dissolved with the slave's consent. In this case, the slave's consent is assumed because she benefits: she keeps any future earnings (since we have maintained her kitabah for the right to earnings) and receives freedom without further payment. Thus, logically, she would agree to the dissolution of the contract.

Key Terms
بدل کتابت (badal kitabah): The payment promised by the mukatab in exchange for freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) She must still pay the full amount
B) The payment is waived entirely
C) She must pay half
D) The contract is transferred to her heirs

Key Takeaway
The master's emancipation of a mukatabah immediately frees the slave and nullifies the remaining kitabah debt.

Kitaba by a Terminally Ill Person: Dispute on Payment When the Kitaba Exceeds Value with Delay
Intra-Hanafi dispute on immediate payment in marad al-mawt kitaba with deferment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the Sahibayn and Imam Muhammad regarding the immediate payment required from a mukatab when the kitaba amount with deferment exceeds the slave's value, and explain the rational arguments (dalil aqli) behind each position.

Core Idea
The deferment (ta'jil) is a relinquishment; the dispute is whether it counts against the one-third of the full kitaba sum or only the slave's value.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, we encounter a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about the rights of heirs when a terminally ill master grants a kitaba contract with a deferred price that exceeds the slave's market value. The Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad disagree on how to calculate the portion payable immediately. By analyzing their arguments, you will understand how the principles of badal (substitute) and isqat (relinquishment) interact in marad al-mawt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Dispute Point
Read the opening: a terminally ill man makes his slave (worth 1000) a mukatab for 2000 payable in a year. He dies with no other property; heirs reject the deferment. The dispute: how much must the slave pay immediately?
The dispute arises because the contract includes both an excess above value and a deferment.
2
State the Sahibayn's Position and Their Reasoning
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule: the slave pays two-thirds of the full 2000 immediately (≈1333.33) and the remainder at term, or else returns to slavery. Their reasoning: the entire named sum is badal al-raqaba; heirs' right attaches to the badal as it attaches to the mubdal. Ta'jil is an isqat, so it must be limited to the one-third that the sick person can donate.
They analogize to a sale: if a sick man sells a house worth 1000 for 3000 with deferment, the buyer pays two-thirds of the full price immediately.
3
State Imam Muhammad's Position and His Reasoning
Muhammad holds: the slave pays two-thirds of the value (≈666.67) immediately and the balance at term. His reasoning: the sick person could have forgone the excess entirely by making kitaba at value, so he can also defer it. This is like khul' where he could divorce without compensation.
He distinguishes khul' from a sale because in khul' the compensation is not a property substitute, so heirs have no claim on it.
4
Compare the Analogies Used
Sahibayn use the sale analogy to show that the full price is badal; Muhammad uses the khul' analogy to show that the excess can be forgiven/deferred. The key difference: in a sale, the price directly substitutes the asset; in khul', the compensation does not substitute the marriage (mubdal).
This distinction determines whether the heir's right extends to the full price or only to the value portion.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: under Sahibayn the slave pays 1333.33 immediately; under Muhammad only 666.67. This affects the slave's chance of freedom and the heirs' immediate return.
The mufta bihi is the view of the Sahibayn, though both are recorded in the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و اذا كاتب المريض عبده على الفي درهم الى سنة وقيمته الف ثم مات ولا مال له غيره ولم يجز الورثة فانه يؤدى ثلثى الالفين حالا والباقى الى اجله او يرد رقيقا عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف وعند محمد يؤدى ثلثى الألف حالا والباقى الى اجله لان له ان يترك الزيادة بان يكاتيه على قيمته فله ان يؤخرها فصار كما اذا خالع المريض امرأته على الف الى سنة جاز لان له ان يطلقها بغير بدل لهما ان جميع المسمى بدل الرقبة حتى أجرى عليها احكام الابدال وحق الورثة متعلق بالمبدل فكذا بالبدل والتاجيل اسقاط معنى فيعتبر من ثلث الجميع بخلاف الخلع لان البدل فيه لا يقابل المال فلم يتعلق حق الورثة بالمبدل فلا يتعلق بالبدل ونظير هذا اذا باع المريض داره بثلاثة آلاف الى سنة وقيمتها الف ثم مات ولم يُجز الورثة فعندهما يقال للمشترى اذ ثلثى جميع الثمن حالا والثلث الى اجله والا فانقض البيع وعنده يعتبر الثلث بقدر القيمة لا فيما زاد عليه لما بينا من المعنى.
He said: If a sick person makes a kitaba contract with his slave for two thousand dirhams payable after one year, and the slave's value is one thousand, then he dies and has no other property, and the heirs do not approve the term, then he (the slave) pays two-thirds of the two thousand immediately and the remainder at its term, or he returns to being a slave according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And according to Muhammad, he pays two-thirds of the one thousand immediately and the remainder at its term, because he (the sick person) had the right to forgo the excess by making a kitaba at the slave's value, so he also has the right to delay it. Thus it is like when a sick person divorces his wife by khul' for one thousand payable after one year—it is valid because he had the right to divorce her without compensation. For them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the entire named sum is the substitute for the slave's person (raqaba), so the rulings of substitution apply to it. The heirs' right is attached to the thing substituted (mubdal), so likewise to the substitute (badal). Giving a term (ta'jil) is a meaning of dropping rights, so it is considered from one-third of the whole. Contrary to khul', because there the substitute does not correspond to property, so the heirs' right was not attached to the thing substituted, hence not attached to the substitute. An analogy for this is if a sick person sold his house for three thousand payable after one year, its value being one thousand, then he dies and the heirs do not approve the term. According to them (the two), the buyer is told: pay two-thirds of the entire price immediately and one-third at its term, otherwise the sale is rescinded. According to him (Muhammad), the third is considered according to the value, not beyond that, for the reason we mentioned.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a kitaba contract made by a terminally ill person (marid) when the amount of kitaba exceeds the slave's value and includes a deferred payment. The scenario: a sick man makes his slave (worth 1000 dirhams) a mukatab for 2000 dirhams payable in one year. The master dies, leaving only this slave as his estate. The heirs do not approve the deferment; they want immediate payment. The ruling differs between the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the slave must pay two-thirds of the full 2000 immediately and the remaining one-third at the end of the year; otherwise, he becomes a slave again. Imam Muhammad holds that the slave only needs to pay two-thirds of the slave's value (i.e., of 1000) immediately, and the remainder of that third at the term. The reasoning: The Sahibayn consider the entire named sum as the substitute for the slave's person (badal al-raqaba). The heirs have a claim on the slave's person since it is part of the estate, so they also have a claim on its substitute. Giving a term is equivalent to relinquishing a right, so its effect is limited to the one-third of the total amount that the sick person can dispose of. Imam Muhammad argues that the sick person could have chosen to make a kitaba at the slave's value without excess, so he has the right to forgive the excess. Similarly, he can also defer payment of the excess without affecting the heirs' rights. The analogy with khul' (divorce for compensation) and a sale with deferred price supports Muhammad's position in his view, but the Sahibayn distinguish khul' because the compensation is not for property, so heirs have no claim on it. The sale analogy is presented: if a sick person sells a house worth 1000 for 3000 payable in a year, and the heirs do not accept the term, the Sahibayn say the buyer must pay two-thirds of the full price immediately and one-third later, or the sale is rescinded. Muhammad says only the portion equal to the value's one-third is considered. This ikhtilaf illustrates the different approaches to the rights of heirs in marad al-mawt transactions with excess and deferment.

Key Terms
البدل (al-badal): The consideration (price) in the kitaba contract, which here is the entire 2000 dirhams.
المبدل (al-mubdal): The thing substituted, i.e., the slave's person (raqaba) which is part of the estate.
التأجيل (al-ta'jil): Deferment of payment; considered a form of isqat (relinquishment) of a right to immediate payment.
المحاباة (al-muhabah): Favoring the other party by charging less than market value; not directly used here but relevant in the second scenario.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what must the mukatab pay immediately if he was made mukatab for 2000 dirhams on one-year term while his value is 1000 dirhams, and the heirs do not approve the term?
A) 666.67 dirhams
B) 1000 dirhams
C) 1333.33 dirhams (two-thirds of 2000)
D) 2000 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The immediate payment in a marad al-mawt kitaba with deferment and excess over value is two-thirds of the full kitaba amount (Sahibayn, mufta bihi) or two-thirds of the value (Muhammad), depending on whether the entire sum or only the value is considered the substitute.

Kitaba by a Terminally Ill Person: When Amount Is Less Than Value
States the unanimous ruling when kitaba amount is less than slave's value with delay

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a terminally ill person writes kitaba for less than the slave's value with deferment, and explain why both the price reduction and the delay are treated as donations limited to one-third.

Core Idea
When a terminally ill person grants a favor in both price and time, both are gifts from the estate, limited to one-third.

In this lesson, we examine a scenario where a terminally ill master makes his slave a mukatab for an amount less than the slave's value, with payment deferred. The heirs do not approve the deferment. All three Hanafi imams unanimously rule that the slave must pay two-thirds of his value immediately or revert to slavery. This ruling illustrates the principle that both the reduction in price and the benefit of delay are considered donations (muhabah) from the sick person, each counted against the one-third limit of bequests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the elements
Note the kitaba amount (1,000 dirhams), the slave's value (2,000 dirhams), and the deferment (one year).
Both the reduced price and the deferred payment are favors.
2
Recognize the nature of muhabah
Understand that muhabah (favor) here includes two components: the reduction in the principal amount and the benefit of time.
In marad al-mawt, all gifts are treated like bequests.
3
Apply the one-third rule
The total favor cannot exceed one-third of the estate. Since both components are gifts, they are combined and limited to one-third.
The slave's value is the baseline; the favor is the difference between value and the kitaba amount plus the value of deferment.
4
Calculate the required payment
The slave must pay two-thirds of his value immediately. Two-thirds of 2,000 is 1,333.33 dirhams.
If the slave cannot pay, he reverts to slavery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وان كاتبه على الف سنة وقيمته الفان ولم يُجز الورثة يقال له ات ثلثى القيمة حالا او ترد رقيقا في قولهم جميعا لان المحاباة ههنا في القدر والتاخير فاعتبر الثلث فيهما
He said: And if he makes a kitaba with him (the slave) for one thousand (dirhams) payable after one year, and his value is two thousand, and the heirs do not approve (the term), it is said to him (the slave): pay two-thirds of the value immediately, or return to being a slave, in the opinion of all of them (the three imams), because here the favor (muhabah) is in the amount and the deferment, so the third is considered in both.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents a second scenario where a terminally ill person makes his slave a mukatab for an amount less than the slave's value, with a deferred payment. Here, the slave is worth 2000 dirhams, but the kitaba amount is only 1000 dirhams payable in one year. The master dies, leaving only the slave, and the heirs do not accept the deferment. All three imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad—agree unanimously that the slave must pay two-thirds of the slave's value (i.e., 1333.33 dirhams of 2000) immediately, or else he becomes a slave again. The reasoning is that here the favor (muhabah) exists in two respects: first in the principal amount (charging less than the value), and second in the deferment (giving time). Both constitute a donation from the sick person, so both must be limited to one-third of the estate. If the total favor (the reduction from value to kitaba amount plus the benefit of deferment) exceeds one-third, the excess is invalid. The calculation takes the slave's value as the baseline and requires immediate payment of two-thirds of that value, effectively limiting the favor to one-third of the estate. This case is simpler because it involves only a reduction in amount and a delay, both of which are treated as gifts.

Key Terms
المحاباة (al-muhabah): Favoring the slave by charging less than the market value and giving time; considered a gift from the sick person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Pay 1000 dirhams immediately
B) Pay two-thirds of the value (1333.33 dirhams) immediately
C) Pay 500 dirhams immediately
D) Pay all 2000 dirhams immediately

Key Takeaway
In kitaba by a terminally ill person, when both the price and the deferment constitute favors, the slave must pay two-thirds of his value immediately to comply with the one-third limit on gifts from the estate.

Slave Contracting Kitabah for Himself and Another Slave
Ruling on kitabah contract for two slaves, one absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a slave can contract kitabah for an absent fellow slave by istihsan, and distinguish the qiyas and istihsan positions.

Core Idea
A slave's kitabah for an absent fellow slave is valid by istihsan, making the present slave principal and the absent one dependent.

This passage addresses the validity of a kitabah contract covering two slaves of the same master, one present and one absent. The Hanafi school employs istihsan to validate what qiyas would prohibit, drawing an analogy to a mother's kitabah including her children. Understanding this ruling reveals how the school prioritizes practical facilitation (taysir) over strict analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a slave making a kitabah contract for himself and another absent slave. The ruling by istihsan is that it is valid (sahih) and binding, freeing both upon payment by either.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
By pure qiyas (analogy), the contract would be valid only for the present slave because he has authority over himself, but suspended for the absent slave due to lack of authority over him.
The illa (effective cause) is the presence or absence of wilayah (authority).
3
Analyze the istihsan reasoning
Istihsan (juridical preference) validates the contract by treating the present slave as the principal (asl) and the absent slave as a dependent (tabi'). This is similar to a female slave's kitabah including her children as dependents.
The present slave adds the contract to himself first, making himself the primary party.
4
Derive practical consequences
The present slave is solely liable for the entire payment; the absent slave owes nothing. If either pays, both are freed. The present slave cannot recover from the absent slave.
5
Evaluate the authority level
This istihsan ruling is part of Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi (operative) position. It is a consistent Hanafi view, though other schools may require separate contracts.

قال واذا كاتب العبد عن نفسه وعن عبد آخر لمولاه وهو غائب فان ادى الشاهد او الغائب عتقاء ومعنى المسألة ان يقول العبد كاتبنى بالف درهم على نفسى وعلى فلان الغائب وهذه الكتابة جائزة استحسان وفى القياس يصح على نفسه لولايته عليها ويتوقف فى حق الغائب لعدم الولاية عليه وجه الاستحسان از الحاضر باضافة العقد الى نفسه ابتداءً جعل نفسه فيه اصلا والغائب تبعا والكتابة على هذا الوجه مشروع كالامة اذا كوتبت دخل اولادها فى كتابتها تبعا حتى عتقوا بادائها وليس عليهم من البدل شيء واذا امكر تصحيحه على هذا الوجه يتفرد به الحاضر فله ان يأخذه بكل البدل لان البدل عليه لكونه اصيلا فيه وا يكون على الغائب من البدل شيء لانه تبع فيه ترجمہ اگر کتابت ٹھہرائی غلام نے اپنی طرف سے اور اپنے آقا کے ایک غلام کی طرف سے جو غائب ہے۔ پس اگر ادا کر دیا حاضر غائب نے تو دونوں آزاد ہو جائیں گے۔ مسئلہ کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ غلام یہ کہے مجھے ہزار درہم کے عوض میری اور فلاں غائب کی ذات مکاتب کر دے یہ کتابت استحساناً جائز ہے اور قیاس میں صرف اس کی ذات پر صحیح ہے اپنی ذات پر ولایت کی وجہ سے اور غائب کے مق یہ موقوف ہے اس پر ولایت نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ وجہ استحسان یہ ہے کہ حاضر غلام نے ابتداء عقد کو اپنی جانب مضاف کر کے خود کو اصل) غائب کو تابع ٹھہرایا ہے اور کتابت ایسے طور پر مشروع ہے جیسے باندی اگر مکاتب کی گئی تو اسکی اولا د تبعا ناس کی کتابت میں داخل ہو جا
If a slave makes a kitabah for himself and for another slave of his master who is absent, and the present slave or the absent one pays, both are freed. The meaning: the slave says 'Make me a mukatab for one thousand dirhams for myself and for so-and-so the absent one.' This kitabah is permissible by istihsan. By qiyas, it is valid for himself because he has authority over himself, and it is suspended regarding the absent one because he has no authority over him. The reason for istihsan: the present slave initially adds the contract to himself, making himself the principal (asl) and the absent one a dependent (tabi'). Kitabah in this way is legitimate, just as when a female slave is made mukataba, her children enter in her kitabah as dependents until they are freed by her payment, and nothing is owed by them. If it is possible to validate it in this way, the present slave is solely responsible; he takes the entire payment because it is due on him as the principal, and nothing is due from the absent one because he is a dependent.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where a slave (present) contracts kitabah not only for himself but also for another slave belonging to the same master who is absent. The ruling: by istihsan, this contract is valid and both slaves are freed if either the present slave or the absent slave pays the full amount. By qiyas, the contract is valid only for the present slave (due to his capacity over himself) and suspended regarding the absent slave due to lack of authority. The istihsan reasoning is based on making the present slave the principal (asl) and the absent slave a dependent (tabi'), analogous to a female slave's kitabah including her children as dependents. The present slave is solely liable for the payment because he is the principal, and the absent slave owes nothing, being a dependent. This is a practical facilitation to allow a single contract to cover multiple slaves.

Key Terms
اصل (asl): The principal in the contract, the one primarily liable and upon whom the contract is built.
تابع (tabi'): A dependent who enters the contract by virtue of being linked to the principal, without independent liability.
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference used to validate this contract despite the qiyas objection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi istihsan ruling in this passage, if the present slave pays the full kitabah amount, what is the status of the absent slave?
A) He remains a slave because he did not pay.
B) He is freed, but must reimburse the present slave half the amount.
C) He is freed and owes nothing to the present slave.
D) He is not affected by the contract because he was absent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a slave's kitabah for an absent fellow slave by istihsan, treating the present slave as principal and the absent as dependent, freeing both upon payment.

Kitabah Including an Absent Slave: The Istihsan Exception
Ruling with proof: Kitabah for self and absent slave, using istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kitabah for a present and absent slave, explain the istihsan proof using the analogy with a mother's kitabah, and distinguish it from the qiyas alternative.

Core Idea
A valid kitabah contract can include an absent slave as a dependent by istihsan, overriding the qiyas that would suspend it.

Kitabah (manumission contract) normally requires the slave's consent and authority over himself. But what if the present slave includes an absent fellow slave? The Hanafi school, using istihsan (juristic preference), validates the contract for both, based on a powerful analogy. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a kitabah contract where a present slave says: 'Make me a mukatab for 1000 dirhams on myself and on so-and-so (absent).' State the ruling: by istihsan it is valid for both; by qiyas it would be valid only for the present and suspended for the absent.
2
Locate the dalil – istihsan
The Hanafi proof is istihsan, which overrides qiyas. The specific evidence (wajh al-dalalah) is the analogy with a female slave's kitabah: when a mother is made mukatabah, her children automatically enter as dependents and are freed with her payment.
This shows that dependency (taba'iyyah) is recognized in kitabah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In the female slave case, the children are included without their own contract because they are dependents. Here, the absent slave is also a dependent (taba') of the present slave, as they share the same master and the present slave initiates the contract. Thus, inclusion by analogy is valid.
The present slave is the principal (asl), the absent is the dependent (taba').
4
Note the ikhtilaf – the opposing qiyas
The three Imams (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad) and some Hanafis follow qiyas: no authority over absent, so contract should be suspended regarding the absent, pending his acceptance or a separate contract.
They argue the present slave cannot bind the absent one.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi because istihsan allows a more beneficial outcome (both slaves freed) without harming the master. The master still gets the full payment from the principal, and the absent obtains freedom without extra burden.
The tarjih is based on the principle of taysir (facilitation) and the strength of the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و اذا كاتب العبد عن نفسه وعن عبد آخر لمولاه وهو غائب فان ادى الشاهد او الغائب عتقاء ومعنى المسألة ان يقول العبد كاتبنى بالف درهم على نفسى وعلى فلان الغائب وهذه الكتابة جائزة استحسان وفى القياس يصح على نفسه لولايته عليها ويتوقف فى حق الغائب لعدم الولاية عليه وجه الاستحسان از الحاضر باضافة العقد الى نفسه ابتداءً جعل نفسه فيه اصلا والغائب تبعا والكتابة على هذا الوجه مشروع كالامة اذا كوتبت دخل اولادها فى كتابتها تبعا حتى عتقوا بادائها وليس عليهم من البدل شيء واذا امكر تصحيحه على هذا الوجه يتفرد به الحاضر فله ان يأخذه بكل البدل لان البدل عليه لكونه اصيلا فيه وا يكون على الغائب من البدل شيء لانه تبع فيه
قال وايهما ادى عتقا ويجبر المولى على القبول اما الحاضر فلان البدل عليه واما النائب فلانه ينال به شرف الحرية وان لم يكن البدل عليه وصار كمعير الرهن اذا ادى الدين يجبر المرتهن على القبول لحاجته الى استخلاص عينه وان لم يكن الدين عليه. قال وايهما ادى لا يرجع على صاحبه لان الحاضر قضى دينا عليه والغائب متبرع به غير مضطر اليه قال وليس للمولى ان يأخذ العبد الغائب بشيء لما بينا فان قبل العبد الغائب او لم يقبل فليس ذلك منه بشيء والكتابة لازمة للشاهد لان الكتابة نافذة عليه من غير قبول الغائب فلا يتغير بقبوله كمن كفل من غيره بغير امره فبلغه فاجازه لا يتغير حكمه حتى لو ادى لا يرجع عليه كذا هذا.
He said: If a slave contracts kitabah for himself and for another slave of his master who is absent, and the present one or the absent one pays, both are freed. The meaning of the case is that the slave says: 'Let me be a mukatab for one thousand dirhams on myself and on so-and-so the absent one.' This kitabah is valid by istihsan. In qiyas, it is valid on himself because he has authority over himself, but it is suspended regarding the absent one due to lack of authority. The reason for istihsan is that the present slave, by initially adding the contract to himself, made himself the principal and the absent one a dependent. Kitabah in this manner is legislated, just like if a female slave is made a mukatabah and her children enter into her kitabah as dependents until they are freed by her payment, and nothing of the payment is due from them. When it is possible to validate it in this manner, the present slave is unique in it, so the master may take the entire payment from him because the payment is upon him as he is the principal, and nothing of the payment is upon the absent one because he is a dependent. He said: Whichever of them pays, they are freed, and the master is compelled to accept. As for the present one, because the payment is upon him. As for the absent one, because he obtains the honor of freedom through it even though the payment is not upon him, and it becomes like the case of a lender of a pledge (mu'ir al-rahn) who pays the debt: the pledgee is compelled to accept due to his need to retrieve his specific property even though the debt was not upon him. He said: Whichever of them pays does not have recourse against the other because the present one discharged a debt that was upon him, and the absent one is a volunteer not compelled. He said: The master may not take anything from the absent slave for what we have stated. If the absent slave accepts or does not accept, that is of no consequence, and the kitabah is binding on the present slave because the kitabah takes effect on him without the acceptance of the absent one; it is not changed by his acceptance, just as if someone stands surety for another without his permission, and the news reaches him and he accepts it, his ruling does not change: even if he pays, he does not have recourse. This is likewise.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof about a slave who makes a kitabah contract for himself and another absent slave of the same master. The author clarifies both the qiyas (analogical) position and the istihsan (juristic preference) position, followed by several subsidiary rulings. The passage type is a ruling with proof, specifically an inter-madhhab dispute where the Hanafi school (by istihsan) holds the contract valid for both, while the opposing schools (the three Imams) follow qiyas and hold it valid only for the present slave. The key proof for the Hanafi position is the analogy with a female slave whose children automatically enter her kitabah as dependents. This analogy establishes that a mukatab can include dependents under his contract. The sub-rulings cover payment consequences: if either the present or absent slave pays the full kitabah amount, both are freed and the master is compelled to accept payment. The one who pays cannot seek reimbursement from the other because the principal paid his own debt and the absent paid voluntarily. The absent slave's acceptance or rejection is irrelevant because the contract is binding on the present slave alone from the outset. This protects the master's right to receive the full payment from the principal while granting freedom to both through a valid istihsan-based contract.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas, based on a stronger evidence or custom. Here, it validates the kitabah for both slaves.
قياس (qiyās): Analogical reasoning that would render the contract valid only for the present slave and suspended for the absent one.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered a kitābah contract with his master to earn freedom by paying a specified sum.
تبع (taba'): Dependent; the absent slave is considered a dependent (taba') of the present slave in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what happens if the absent slave pays the full kitabah amount without the present slave's involvement?
A) Only the absent slave is freed, and the present slave remains a slave.
B) Both slaves are freed, and the master is compelled to accept the payment.
C) The payment is not accepted, and the contract becomes void.
D) The absent slave has recourse to recover the payment from the present slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a kitabah contract can validly include an absent slave as a dependent by istihsan, overriding the qiyas that would suspend it, and payment by either frees both.

Female Slave's Kitabah Including Her Young Children
Ruling on validity of kitabah including children

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a female slave's kitabah validly includes her young children and explain the Hanafi istihsān reasoning that supports it.

Core Idea
A female slave's kitabah validly includes her young children as dependents, by istihsān, because she is the principal and they are her dependents.

This lesson examines the validity of a kitabah contract when a female slave includes her young children in the agreement. The Hanafi school, through istihsān, permits this extension, treating her as the principal (asīl) and her children as dependents (tabaʿ). We will explore the reasoning and the practical consequences for all parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a female slave contracting kitabah for herself and her two young sons. The ruling is that it is valid (jāʾiz). Note the hukm level is sahih (valid).
2
Understand the principal/dependent distinction
The mother is the principal (asīl) in the contract, and her children are dependents (tabaʿ). This determines the legal consequences: the children are included without separate negotiation.
This mirrors the earlier case of a slave contracting for himself and an absent slave.
3
Trace the istihsān reasoning
The ruling is based on istihsān (juristic preference). By qiyas, the contract would be restricted to the mother only, but istihsān allows inclusion because the mother is more entitled to include her children than a stranger would be.
The analogy (qiyas jaliy) is drawn to the previous case where the principal/dependent relationship justified inclusion.
4
Note the practical consequences
If any one of them (mother or a son) pays the full kitabah amount, all are freed. The master is compelled to accept payment from any of them, and there is no right of recourse among them.
This ensures the contract is enforceable and benefits all parties.
5
Compare with the previous analogous case
The first case involved a slave contracting for himself and an absent slave. Here, the mother is analogous to the principal slave, and her young children are analogous to the absent slave. The mother's relationship is even stronger, reinforcing istihsān.

قال واذا كاتبت الامة عن نفسها وعن ابنين لها صغيرين فهو جائز وايهم ادى لم يرجع على صاحبه ويجبر المولى على القبول ويعتقون لانها جعلت نفسها اصيلا فى الكتابة واولادها تبعا على ما بينا فى المسألة الاولى و هي اولى بذلك من الاجنبـ
He said: If a female slave contracts kitabah for herself and for two young sons of hers, it is valid. Whichever of them pays does not have recourse against the other, and the master is compelled to accept, and they all become free because she made herself the principal in the kitabah and her children as dependents, as we explained in the first issue. And she is more entitled to that than a stranger.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a female slave (amah) who enters a kitabah contract covering herself and her two young children. The ruling states that this contract is valid (jā'iz) on the same principle as the previous case: the mother is the principal (asīl) and her children are dependents (taba'). This is an application of the same istihsān reasoning. The passage type is a ruling with proof, drawing explicit analogy to the earlier case of the slave contracting for himself and an absent slave. The mother is more entitled to include her children than a stranger would be, strengthening the analogy. The sub-rulings mirror those of the first case: if any one of them pays the full kitabah amount, all are freed, the master is compelled to accept, and there is no right of recourse between them. This aligns with the Hanafi position that a kitabah contract can validly include dependents by istihsān.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitabah): A contract of manumission by which a slave purchases freedom by paying installments to the master.
أمة (amah): A female slave who becomes a mukatabah.
أصيل (asīl): Principal; the primary contracting party.
تبع (tabaʿ): Dependent; someone included in a contract by virtue of their relationship to the principal.
استحسان (istihsān): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that overrides strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable outcome.

Kitabah of Present Slave Including Absent Subordinate
Hanafi istihsan ruling on kitabah including an absent slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on kitabah for an absent slave by istihsan and distinguish it from the qiyas position of the Three Imams.

Core Idea
Istihsan validates the absent slave's inclusion as subordinate, compelling the master to accept payment from either.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah presents a nuanced dispute between the Hanafi school and the Three Imams. At issue is whether a present slave can validly contract kitabah on behalf of an absent slave. The Hanafi position, based on istihsan, recognizes a valid contract through subordination, analogous to a mother including her children. Understanding this ruling illuminates the Hanafi methodology of preferring istihsan over strict qiyas in transactions involving benefit and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Core Question
A present slave asks his master to make both himself and an absent slave mukatab for a lump sum. The question: is the contract valid for the absent slave without his consent?
The present slave initiates the contract; the absent slave is not present to consent.
2
State the Qiyas Position (Three Imams)
According to qiyas, the present slave has no authority over the absent slave, so the contract should be suspended until the absent slave consents. Only the present slave is bound.
This is the default ruling by analogy to general contract law.
3
State the Hanafi Istihsan Position
By istihsan, the contract is valid for both. The present slave is the principal (asil), and the absent is the subordinate (tabi'), akin to a mother including her children. The master must accept payment from either.
Istihsan overrides qiyas due to established practice and benefit.
4
Analyze the Dalil for Istihsan
The analogy to a female slave's kitabah for her children establishes subordination. Further, the absent slave's payment brings freedom, so compelling the master is like compelling a mortgagee to accept payment from the lender of a pledged item (mu'ir al-rahn).
Both analogies demonstrate that benefit justifies compelling acceptance.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Difference)
Under qiyas, the absent slave's kitabah is invalid; the master can refuse his payment. Under istihsan, the contract is binding, and the master must accept payment from either, freeing both. The paying slave cannot recover from the other.
This affects the master's obligation and the absent slave's path to freedom.

ہے یہاں تک کہ باندی کے ادا کرنے سے اولاد بھی آزاد ہو جاتی ہے اور اولاد پر بدل سے کچھ واجب نہیں ہوتا جب اس طور پر عقد کی تصیح ممکن ہے تو حاضر غلام اس میں منفرد ہو گا پس آقا کل بدل کا مواخذہ اسی سے کرے گا کیونکہ بدل اسی پر ہے اس کے اصیل ہونے کی وجہ سے اور غائب پر بدل میں سے کچھ نہ ہو گا کیونکہ وہ اس میں تابع ہے۔
تشريح .. قوله واذا كاتب العبد .... الخ - ایک شخص کے دو غلام ہیں جن میں سے ایک حاضر ہے دوسرا غائب۔ جو غلام حاضر ہے اس نے آقا سے کہا کہ مجھ کو اور فلاں غائب غلام کو ایک ہزار پر مکاتب کر دے۔ آقا نے مکاتب کر دیا اور حاضر غلام نے قبول کر لیا تو از روئے قیاس یہ کتابت صرف حاضر غلام کے حق میں جائز ہونی چاہئے۔ کیونکہ اس کو صرف اپنی قناعت پر ولایت حاصل ہے اور غائب غلام کی کتابت موقوف ہونی چاہیے۔ کیونکہ غائب پر اس کو کوئی ولایت نہیں ہے۔ چنانچہ ائمہ ثلاثہ اسی کے قائل ہیں۔ لیکن استحسانا دونوں کی کتابت صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ حاضر غلام نے عقد کتابت کو اولاً اپنی طرف منسوب کر کے خود اصیل بنایا ہے اور غائب کو اپنا تابع کیا ہے۔ اور اس طرح کی کتابت مشروع ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر کوئی باندی مکاتبہ کی گئی تو اس کی اولا د تبعا اس کی کتابت میں داخل ہو جاتی ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ باندی کے بدل کتابت ادا کرنے پر یہ اولاد بھی آزاد ہو جاتی ہے اور اولاد پر بدل کتابت سے کچھ واجب نہیں ہوتا۔ بہر کیف جب بطریق تبع عقد مذکور کو سی ٹھہر انا [؟] ممکن ہے تو حاضر غلام اس کو تنہا پورا کرنے والا ہوا۔ پس آقا کو اختیار ہوگا کہ وہ پورے بدل کا مواخذہ اسی حاضر غلام سے کرے۔ غائب غلام پر کچھ واجب نہ ہوگا کیونکہ وہ اس معاملہ میں تابع ہے اصل نہیں ہے۔
ان میں سے جس نے ادا کر دئیے آزاد ہو جائیں گے اور آقا کو قبول کرنے پر مجبور کیا جائے گا . قال وايهما ادى عتـقـا ويجبر المولى على القبول اما الحاضر فلان البدل عليه واما النائب فلانه ينال به شرف الحرية وان لم يكن البدل عليه وصار كمعير الرهن اذا ادى الدين يجبر المرتهن على القبول لحاجته الى استخلاص عينه وان لم يكن الدين عليه. قال وايهما ادى لا يرجع على صاحبه لان الحاضر قضى دينا عليه والغائب متبرع به غير مضطر اليه قال وليس للمولى ان يأخذ العبد الغائب بشيء لما بينا فان قبل العبد الغائب او لم يقبل فليس ذلك منه بشيء والكتابة لازمة للشاهد لان الكتابة نافذة عليه من غير قبول الغائب فلا يتغير بقبوله كمن كفل من غيره بغير امره فبلغه فاجازه لا يتغير حكمه حتى لو ادى لا يرجع عليه كذا هذا. قال واذا كاتبت الامة عن نفسها وعن ابنين لها صغيرين فهو جائز وايهم ادى لم يرجع على صاحبه ويجبر المولى على القبول ويعتقون لانها جعلت نفسها اصيلا فى الكتابة واولادها تبعا على ما بينا فى الم ألة الاولی و هی اولی بذلك من الاجنبـ
نبی
ترجمہ اور ان میں سے جس نے ادا کیا دونوں آزاد ہو جائیں گے اور آقا کو قبول کرنے پر مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ بہر حال حاضر سو اس لئے کہ بدل اسی پر ہے۔ رہا غائب سو اس لئے کہ وہ اس کے ذریعہ شرف حریت پائیگا۔ اگر چہ بدل اس پر نہیں ہے اور یہ رہن عاریت دینے والے کی طرح ہو گیا کہ جب وہ دین ادا کرے تو مرتہن کو اسکے قبول کرنے پر مجبور کیا جاتا ہے۔ کیونکہ اس کو اپنا مال عین چھڑانے کی ضرورت ہے اگر چہ اس پر قرضہ نہیں ہے اور ان میں سے جو کوئی ادا کرے وہ دوسرے سے نہیں لے سکتا۔ کیونکہ حاضر نے ایسا دین چکا یا جو اس کے ذمہ تھا اور غائب اس میں احسان کنندہ ہے مجبور نہیں ہے۔ اور آقا غائب غلام سے کسی چیز کا مواخذہ نہیں کر سکتا۔ پھر غائب غلام نے عقد قبول کیا سو یہ اس کی طرف سے کچھ نہیں اور کتابت حاضر کے ذمہ لازم ہے۔ کیونکہ کتابت اس پر نافذ ہوچکی غائب کے قبول کئے بغیر تو غائب کے قبول کرنے سے معتبر نہ ہوگی۔ جیسے کسی نے غیر کی طرف سے اس کے حکم کے بغیر کفالت کی اور مکفول عنہ کو اس کی خبر پہنچی اور اس نے اجازت دے دی تو اس کا حکم متغیر نہیں ہوتا۔ یہاں تک کہ اگر کفیل نے ادا کر دیا تو وہ مکفول عنہ سے نہیں لے سکتا۔ ایسے ہی یہاں ہے۔ اگر کسی باندی نے اپنی اور اپنے دو چھوٹے بچوں کی ذات سے کتابت کی تو یہ جائز ہے۔ اور ان میں سے جو بھی ادا کر لے وہ دوسرے سے نہیں لے سکتا اور آقا کو قبول کرنے پر مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ اور وہ سب آزاد ہو جائیں گے۔ کیونکہ باندی نے اپنی ذات کو کتابت میں اصیل اور اپنی اولاد کو تابع ٹھہرایا ہے۔ جیسا کہ ہم نے سابق مسئلہ میں بیان کیا بلکہ ماں یہ نسبت اجنبی کے اولی ہے۔ تشریح قوله وايهما ادى .... الخ - حاضر و غائب دونوں غلاموں میں سے جو کوئی بدل کتابت ادا کرے آقا کو جبز الینا پڑے گا اور وہ دونوں آزاد ہو جائیں گے۔ مگر یہ استحسان ہے قیاس یہ ہے کہ غائب کے بارے میں آقا کو قبول کرنے پر مجبور نہ کیا جائے ۔ چنانچہ ائمہ ثلاثہ اسی کے قائل ہیں۔ اس لئے کہ غائب تو محض متبرع ہے۔ بدل کتابت کا مطالبہ اس سے متعلق نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ نہ اس کو ادائیگی کا حکم ہے نہ وہ ادائیگی میں معطر ہے۔ لیکن استحسانا اس لئے مجبور کیا جائیگا۔ تا کہ غائب کے ادا کرنے سے دونوں آزاد ہو جائیں۔ سوال جب مسئلہ کے آغاز میں یہ کہہ چکے فان ادی الشاهد او الغائب عتقا تو پھر یہاں وايهما ادی عنقا " کہنا تکرار محض ہے۔

The book of Mukatab. ... When it is possible to validate the contract in this manner, then the present slave becomes the sole performer. So the master will demand the entire payment from him because the payment is upon him due to his being principal, and nothing of the payment will be upon the absent because he is subordinate in this. Commentary on his saying: 'And when the slave makes a kitabah...' - A person has two slaves, one present and one absent. The present slave said to the master: 'Make both me and that absent slave mukatab for one thousand.' The master made the kitabah and the present slave accepted. According to qiyas, this kitabah should be valid only for the present slave, because he has authority only over his own consent, and the kitabah of the absent slave should be suspended because he has no authority over the absent. Thus the Three Imams hold this view. But by istihsan, the kitabah of both is valid, because the present slave first attributed the contract to himself, making himself the principal and the absent his subordinate. And such a kitabah is legislated. For example, if a female slave is made mukatabah, then her children are included in her kitabah as subordinates, to the extent that when she pays the kitabah price, the children also become free, and nothing of the price is obligatory upon the children. ... Whoever among them pays, both become free, and the master will be compelled to accept. As for the present, because the payment is upon him; as for the absent, because he attains the honor of freedom through it, even though the payment is not upon him, and it becomes like the lender of a collateral (mu'ir al-rahn) when he pays the debt, the mortgagee is compelled to accept due to his need to redeem his property, even though the debt was not upon him. ... And if a female slave makes a kitabah for herself and her two young sons, it is valid, and whoever among them pays cannot seek reimbursement from the others, and the master will be compelled to accept, and they become free, because she made herself principal in the kitabah and her children subordinates, as we explained in the first case, and she is more entitled to that than a stranger.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah addresses a scenario where a slave who is present (hazir) asks his master to make both himself and an absent slave (gha'ib) mukatab for a fixed sum. The author explains the difference between qiyas and istihsan. According to qiyas, which the Three Imams (likely Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) adhere to, the contract is valid only regarding the present slave because he cannot bind the absent one. The absent slave's kitabah would be suspended pending his consent. However, the Hanafi school adopts istihsan and declares the kitabah valid for both. The reasoning is that the present slave treats himself as the principal (asil) and the absent as a subordinate (tabi'), analogous to a mother who includes her children in her kitabah. This subordination is legally recognized, as seen in the case of a female slave whose children are automatically included in her kitabah without separate payment. Consequently, the master can demand the full payment from the present slave, and if either slave pays, both become free. The master is compelled to accept payment from either, even if the absent slave pays voluntarily, because he attains the honor of freedom. The absent slave's payment is likened to a person who lends an item for use as a pledge (mu'ir al-rahn) and then pays off the debt to redeem his property; the mortgagee must accept. However, the paying slave cannot seek reimbursement from the other—the present slave is paying his own debt, and the absent is a volunteer (mutabarri'). The absentee's subsequent acceptance or refusal is irrelevant because the contract was already binding on the present slave. The passage also covers the case of a female slave making kitabah for herself and her minor children, which is valid by the same principle. The commentary ends with a clarification of why the absent slave is not considered 'compelled' like the mu'ir al-rahn, who can recover from the borrower; here, the absent slave is purely benevolent.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who enters into a contract of kitabah with his master, agreeing to pay a specified sum in exchange for freedom upon payment.
اصیل (asil): The principal party in a contract; here, the present slave who takes primary responsibility for the kitabah payment.
تابع (tabi'): Subordinate; a person included in a contract by virtue of being dependent on the principal, such as children included in their mother's kitabah.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger legal indication, such as established practice or need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view, why is the master compelled to accept payment from the absent slave in the kitabah scenario?
A) Because the absent slave is the principal in the contract.
B) Because the absent slave attains the honor of freedom through payment, similar to a mu'ir al-rahn redeeming his property.
C) Because the absent slave has already consented to the contract.
D) Because the master has no right to refuse any payment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates the kitabah of an absent slave by istihsan, treating him as a subordinate of the present principal slave, and compels the master to accept payment from either for the benefit of emancipation.

Ruling on Joint Mukatab Slaves: Present and Absent
Explains the ruling when one of two joint mukatab slaves pays the kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position on joint kitabah and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in compelling the master to accept payment from either slave.

Core Idea
In a joint kitabah, if either slave pays, the master must accept—istihsan facilitates freedom over strict analogy.

When two slaves jointly contract kitabah, one present and one absent, what happens if the absent slave pays the full price? The Hanafi school, using istihsan, compels the master to accept, freeing both. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning, the opposing qiyas view, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the master is compelled to accept payment from either slave, and both are freed. This is the mufta bihi view.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Three Imams (likely referring to early scholars outside the Hanafi school or a minority within) hold that the master is not compelled regarding the absent slave based on qiyas.
The source says 'ئمہ ثلاثہ' (Three Imams).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Their dalil is qiyas: the absent slave is a volunteer not liable for the kitabah, so the master should not be forced to accept his payment.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school uses istihsan: compelling the master facilitates freedom for both slaves, and the absent is not a complete stranger but a dependent in the contract, similar to a child. Also analogized to a lender of collateral (mu'ir rahn) who pays to redeem his property.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: on the Hanafi view, the absent slave's payment frees both immediately; on the opposing view, the master could refuse and only the present's payment would free both.
Also: the paying slave cannot recover from the other.

اور آقا کو غائب سے بدل کتابت کے مطالبہ کا کچھ اختیار نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے کہ وہ حاضر کی کتابت میں تبعا داخل ہے اور اس کے ذمہ کچھ عوض نہیں ہے۔ پھر غائب غلام عقد کتابت قبول کرے یا نہ کرے اس سے کچھ تغیر نہ ہوگا۔ بلکہ عقد کتابت حاضر غلام کے ذمہ باقی رہے گا۔ کیونکہ کتابت تو اس کے ذمہ قبول غائب کے بغیر ہی نافذ ہو چکی تو غائب کا قبول کرنا یا رد کرنا کچھ مؤثر نہ ہوگا۔ اس کی نظیر ایسی ہے جیسے کسی نے دوسرے کی طرف سے اس کے حکم کے بغیر کفالت کر لی۔ پھر مغول عنہ کواس کی خبر پہنچی اور اس نے کفالت کو جائز رکھا تو حکم متغیرنہیں ہوتا بلکہ فیل متبرع ہی باقی رہتا ہے۔ حتی کہ اگر کفیل نے مال کفالت ادا کر دیا تو وہ مکفول عنہ سے واپس نہیں لے سکتا پس جیسے مکفول عنہ کے قبول کرنے سے کفالت میں ایسا تغیر نہیں ہوتا کہ کفیل کو واپس لینے کا اختیار حاصل ہو ۔ ایسے ہی غائب غلام کے قبول کرنے سے کفالت سے وہ مال کا ذمہ دارنہ ہو جائے گا۔
He accepted, so this is nothing from him, and the kitabah is binding upon the present slave. Because the kitabah was executed upon him without the acceptance of the absent slave, so it will not be valid by the acceptance of the absent slave. Like if someone made kafalah on behalf of another without his permission, and the person for whom kafalah was made heard of it and gave permission, then its ruling does not change. Even if the guarantor pays, he cannot recover from the person for whom he guaranteed. Similarly here. If a female slave contracted kitabah for herself and her two young children, it is permissible. And whichever of them pays, he cannot take anything from the other, and the master will be compelled to accept. And all of them will be freed. Because the female slave made herself principal in the kitabah and her children subordinate, as we explained in the previous case. Rather, the mother is more deserving of this status than a stranger. Explanation of his saying: "And whichever of them pays..." - If either of the two slaves, present or absent, pays the kitabah price, the master must accept, and both will be freed. But this is istihsan; by qiyas, the master should not be compelled to accept regarding the absent one. Indeed, the Three Imams hold this view. Because the absent one is merely a volunteer; the demand for the kitabah price is not connected to him. Since neither is he commanded to pay nor is he involved in payment. But by istihsan, he will be compelled so that both are freed by the absent one's payment. Objection: At the beginning of the case, we already said: "If the present or the absent pays, they are freed." So why repeat "And whichever of them pays, they are freed"? Answer: This is a prelude to "And the master will be compelled to accept." His saying: "As for the present..." - If the present slave pays the kitabah price, the master must take it because the kitabah price is a debt upon him. So the master is compelled to accept his payment, and by it the absent slave also becomes free because he is included in the present's kitabah as a dependent, just as the child of a mukatabah is included in her kitabah as a dependent. And regarding the absent one's payment, although the kitabah price is not obligatory upon him, he will obtain the honor of freedom through it. So he is not like a complete stranger. Rather, it is like a person who gave his thing to someone as a loan to be used as collateral. Then the lender wanted to redeem the thing by paying off the debt to the pledgee. The pledgee will be compelled to accept because he needs to redeem his property, even though he is not obliged to pay the debt. His saying: "And whichever of them pays, he cannot take recourse..." - Whichever of the absent or present pays, he cannot take recourse against the other. Because if the present pays, he has discharged his own debt, as he is the original contractor and the debt is upon him, and such a payer has no right to take back. And if the absent pays, he is a volunteer, and a benefactor does not take back from another. Objection: Here, the absent slave is like the lender of collateral who is constrained, and therefore he can recover from the borrower. So why is he said to be not constrained? Answer: He is like the lender of collateral only in that it is permissible for him to pay even though the debt is not upon him. As for being constrained, he is not like the lender of collateral because constraint occurs only when something already possessed is lost. But here this is not the case; rather, he is merely aspiring to obtain freedom. His saying: "And the master has no right..." - The master has no right to demand the kitabah price from the absent slave because he is included in the present's kitabah as a dependent and there is no consideration upon him. Then whether the absent slave accepts the kitabah contract or not, nothing changes. Rather, the kitabah contract remains upon the present slave, because the kitabah was already executed upon him without the absent's acceptance. So the absent's acceptance or rejection has no effect. Its analogy is like someone who made kafalah on behalf of another without his permission. Then the person for whom kafalah was made heard of it and approved the kafalah, but the ruling does not change, and the guarantor remains a volunteer. Even if the guarantor pays the kitabah? (the debt), he cannot recover from the person for whom he guaranteed. Just as the approval of the person for whom kafalah was made does not change the kafalah to give the guarantor the right to recover, similarly, the absent slave's acceptance does not make him liable for the property.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when two slaves, one present and one absent, jointly enter a kitabah contract. The key ruling is that if either pays the full kitabah price, the master is compelled to accept, and both slaves become free. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference), while qiyas (analogy) would require the master not to be compelled for the absent slave since he was not originally liable. The text mentions that the Three Imams (احمد ثلاثہ) hold the qiyas view, but the Hanafi position follows istihsan. The author then explains the rationale: the present slave is the principal, and the absent is subordinate, so the absent's payment benefits himself as well. Further, the ruling that the paying slave cannot recover from the other is justified differently for each: if the present pays, he is discharging his own debt; if the absent pays, he is a volunteer. A comparison is made with a lender of collateral (معیر رہن) who pays to redeem his property, but the difference is that the lender is constrained to preserve his existing property, while the absent slave is only seeking to obtain freedom. The passage ends with the clarification that the master cannot demand payment from the absent slave, and the absent's acceptance or rejection of the contract does not change the binding nature of the kitabah on the present slave, similar to the case of unauthorized suretyship.

Key Terms
کتابت (kitabah): A contract between a master and a slave whereby the slave pays a fixed sum in installments and becomes free upon completion.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas for a more equitable outcome, used here to compel the master to accept payment from the absent slave.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning that would deny forcing the master to accept from the absent slave because the absent is not liable.
ائمہ ثلاثہ (al-a'immah al-thalathah): The Three Imams – likely referring to the founders of the other major Sunni schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) or possibly a minority early Hanafi view. The text treats them as holding the qiyas position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if two slaves jointly enter a kitabah contract and the absent slave pays the full kitabah price, what is the ruling?
A) The master may refuse payment; only the present slave's payment frees both.
B) The master is compelled to accept, and both slaves become free.
C) The absent slave cannot pay because he is not liable.
D) The present slave must also pay for the absent to be freed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, istihsan can override qiyas to achieve a more equitable outcome, as seen in the joint kitabah case where the master is compelled to accept payment from the absent slave to free both.
