Translation of the chapter: On the Death of the Mukatib, His Inability, and the Death of the Master. If the mukatib becomes unable to pay a installment, the judge will look into his situation. If he has some debt that he will collect or some property that will come to him, he should not hurry in declaring him unable, but wait for two or three days for the sake of both parties. He said: 'And when the mukatib becomes unable to pay a installment, the judge looks into his situation. If he has a debt that he will collect or property that will come to him, he should not hasten in declaring him unable, but wait for two or three days for the sake of both parties. And three days is the period set for presenting excuses, like giving the defendant time to present his defense and the debtor time to pay the debt. No more than that is given.' Commentary: His statement 'chapter...' - The reason for mentioning the death of the mukatib, the inability of the mukatib, and the death of the master is obvious, because these matters occur after the contract of kitaba, so their rulings are also mentioned later. His statement 'and when he becomes unable...' - A person made his slave a mukatib on payment of installments of kitaba. Then the slave became unable to pay a installment. If there is hope of getting money from somewhere, the judge should not decide his inability. Rather, he should wait two or three days, because in this there is consideration for both parties (the master and the mukatib). And the period of three days is the period set for presenting excuses. Thus, the defendant is given three days to present his defense against the plaintiff, and the debtor is given three days to pay the debt. Also the period of khiyar is legally three days. So no more than that is given. And the origin of this chapter is the story of Musa and Khidr (peace be upon them), that Khidr said to Musa at the third incident: 'This is a parting between me and you.' If he has no means and the master requests his declaration of inability, the judge declares him unable and annuls the kitaba. 'If he has no means and the master requests his declaration of inability, he declares him unable and annuls the kitaba.' And this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and...
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the story of Musa and Khidr. The chapter discusses the situation when a mukatib (a slave under a kitaba contract who is to be freed upon paying installments) becomes unable to pay an installment. The judge must first investigate: if the mukatib has a debt receivable or property expected, he should wait two or three days to allow him to gather the means. This is for the benefit of both the master and the mukatib. The three-day period is established by precedent: it is the same period given to a defendant to prepare a defense or to a debtor to pay, and it is also the period for khiyar (option) in contracts. The proof for this three-day period is derived from the Quranic story of Musa and Khidr, where at the third incident Khidr said 'This is a parting'—indicating that a third time is the limit. If after waiting the mukatib has no means and the master requests that he be declared unable, the judge must declare him unable and dissolve the kitaba contract. This ruling is according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad. The text ends incomplete ('and...') but the ruling is clear.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukatib): A slave who has made a contract of kitaba with his master to earn and pay for his freedom in installments.
نجم (najm): Installment of the kitaba payment.
تعجیز (ta'jiz): Declaration by the judge that the mukatib is unable to pay, resulting in annulment of the kitaba.
خیار (khiyar): Option period in contracts, here analogized to the three-day waiting period.

Ruling on a Mukatab's Inability to Pay: Waiting Period and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on ta'jiz of a mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the waiting period for a mukatab who misses an installment and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over the number of missed installments required for rescission.

Core Idea
One missed installment suffices for rescission per the majority; Abu Yusuf requires two.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a practical scenario: a mukatab (slave under kitaba) fails to pay an installment. The judge must first evaluate if there is hope of payment before declaring incapacity. Beyond that procedural rule, the passage delves into a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on whether one or two missed installments trigger rescission. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to weigh conflicting proofs within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedural ruling
Read the opening: the judge must wait 2-3 days if the mukatab has a debt to collect or property. Note the rationale: consideration for both parties and the need for a realistic chance to pay.
This waiting period is a fixed procedure, not discretionary.
2
State the majority position on rescission
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that one missed installment suffices for ta'jiz (declaration of incapacity) upon the master's request. Note the key argument: inability to pay one installment shows inability to pay two, and the master's aim (timely payment) is already frustrated.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his proof
Abu Yusuf requires two consecutive missed installments. His dalil is the saying of Ali (RA): 'When two installments become due consecutively... he is returned to slavery.' He also argues that kitaba is a contract of kindness requiring leniency.
The hadith of Ali is narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba and al-Bayhaqi.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
The majority counter that the cause (inability) is already present with one missed installment, and the athar from Ibn Umar (returning a mukatab after one missed installment) contradicts Ali's report, weakening its force.
They also stress that the two-three day wait is not a delay but a necessity for payment to materialize.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah)
Under the majority, the master can demand rescission sooner; under Abu Yusuf, the mukatab gets more time. The mufta bihi is the majority view, so fatwa follows one missed installment.
This dispute has real implications for the timing of freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا عجز المكاتب عن نجم نظر الحاكم فى حاله فان كان له دين يقبضه او مال يقدم عليه لم يعجل بتعجيزه وانتظر عليه اليومين او الثلاثة نظرا للجانبين والثلاث هي المدة التى ضُربت لابلاء الاعذار کامهال الخصم للدفع والمديون للقضاء فلا يزاد عليه فان لم يكن له وجه وطلب المولى تعجيزه عجزه وفسخ الكتابة وهذا عند ابي حنيفة ومحمد وقال ابويوسف لا يُعجزه حتى يتوالى عليه نجمان لقول على اذا توالى على المكاتب نجمان ردّ في الرق علقه بهذا الشرط ولانه عقد ارفاق حتى كان احسنه مؤجله وحالة الوجوب بعد حلول نجم فلابد من امهال مدة استيسارًا واولى المدد ما توافق عليه العاقدان ولهما ان سبب الفسخ قد تحقق وهو العجز لان من عجز عن اداء نجم واحد يكون اعجز عن اداء نجمين وهذا لان مقصود المولى الوصول الى المال عند حلول نجم وقد فات فيفسخ اذا لم يكن راضيا به دونه بخلاف اليومين والثلاثة لانه لابد منها لامكان الاداء فلم يكن تاخيرا والآثار متعارضة فان المروى عن ابن عمران مكاتبة له عجزت عن نجم فردها فسقط الاحتجاج بها

He said: When the mukatab becomes unable (to pay) an installment, the judge looks into his situation. If he has a debt that he can collect or some property that will come to him, he should not be quick to declare him incapable and should wait two or three days, out of consideration for both parties. Three days is the period set for presenting excuses, like giving the defendant time to respond and the debtor time to repay; no more should be added. If he has no means and the master demands his declaration of incapacity, he declares him incapable and rescinds the kitaba. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: He should not declare him incapable until two installments have become due consecutively upon him, because of the saying of Ali: 'When two installments become due consecutively upon the mukatab, he is returned to slavery.' He (Ali) tied it to this condition. And because the contract of kitaba is a contract of kindness, to the extent that the best form is a deferred one, and the obligation arises after the due date of an installment, so a period of leniency must be given for ease, and the best period is that which the two parties agree upon. Their (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's) argument is that the cause of rescission has been realized, namely inability, because one who is unable to pay one installment is even more unable to pay two. This is because the master's aim is to obtain the money at the due date of the installment, and it has been missed, so the contract is rescinded if he is not satisfied with it without that. This differs from the two or three days, because those are necessary to make payment possible, so they are not a delay. The athar (narrations) are conflicting; indeed, it is narrated from Ibn Umar that a mukatab of his became unable to pay an installment, so he returned her to slavery, so the argument (from Ali's hadith) falls. Translation: When the mukatab becomes unable to pay an installment, the judge examines his situation. If he has some debt to collect or some property that will come to him, he should not be hasty in declaring him incapable, but should wait two or three days out of consideration for both parties. Three days is the period set for presenting excuses, such as giving the defendant time to respond and the debtor time to repay; no more than that should be given. Explanation: The chapter is appropriate because these matters come after the contract of kitaba. The ruling: If someone has made his slave a mukatab on installments and he becomes unable to pay, if there is hope of getting money, the judge should not decide on his incapacity but wait two or three days for the benefit of both parties. Three days is the period for presenting excuses; similarly, the option period (khiyar) is three days. The story of Musa and Khidr is the basis: Khidr said on the third occasion, 'This is the parting between me and you.' If the mukatab has no means and the master requests his declaration of incapacity, the judge declares him incapable and rescinds the kitaba. This is according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Imam Abu Yusuf says that he should not declare him incapable until two installments become due consecutively. Explanation: If the mukatab has no way to obtain money and cannot pay even after three days, and the master requests his declaration of incapacity, the judge rules on his incapacity and rescinds the kitaba. This is according to the two. Imam Abu Yusuf says that he should not rule on his incapacity until two consecutive installments are due. Imam Ahmad, Ibn Abi Layla, Sufyan ibn Uyayna, and Hasan ibn Salih also hold this view. The proof is the saying of Ali narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba in al-Musannaf and al-Bayhaqi in al-Sunan: 'When two installments become due consecutively upon the mukatab and he does not pay them, he is returned to slavery.' So Ali tied the declaration of incapacity to this condition, and what is conditional on a condition does not exist before the condition. This is not known through analogy, and what is narrated from him is like what is narrated from him, peace be upon him.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage from the Hidayah discusses the ruling on a mukatab who fails to pay an installment. It is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The passage first states the basic ruling: if a mukatab cannot pay an installment, the judge must assess his situation. If the mukatab has a debt to collect or property to receive, the judge should not immediately declare him incapacitated but should wait two or three days, considering both the master's and the slave's interests. Three days is the standard period for presenting excuses, similar to giving a defendant or debtor time. The judge cannot extend beyond three days. However, if the mukatab has no means and the master demands his declaration of incapacity (ta'jiz), the judge declares him incapacitated and rescinds the kitaba. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, requiring two consecutive missed installments before the judge can declare incapacity. Abu Yusuf relies on a saying of Imam Ali: 'When two installments become due consecutively upon the mukatab, he is returned to slavery.' He also argues that the kitaba is a contract of kindness, so the mukatab deserves leniency and time to arrange payment; the best period is whatever the parties agreed upon. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) counter that the cause of rescission—inability to pay—is already confirmed by missing one installment, because one who cannot pay one is even less able to pay two. The master's aim is to receive payment on time, and that aim is frustrated, so rescission is justified if the master is not satisfied without payment. The two or three day wait is not a delay but a necessary allowance for payment. Furthermore, the athar (reports) are contradictory: the hadith of Ali is countered by an athar from Ibn Umar, who returned his mukatab to slavery after one missed installment, undermining reliance on Ali's report. Thus, the majority view is stronger and is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitaba contract with his master, agreeing to pay installments to earn freedom.
نجم (najm): An installment of the kitaba price; the due date for payment.
تعجيز (ta'jiz): Declaration by the judge that the mukatab is incapable of paying, leading to rescission of the kitaba and return to slavery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), how many missed installments are required before the judge can declare a mukatab incapacitated upon the master's request?
A) One missed installment is sufficient.
B) Two consecutive missed installments are required.
C) Three missed installments are required.
D) The judge has complete discretion without any fixed number.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi madhhab, the relied-upon position is that a mukatab who misses one installment can be declared incapacitated upon the master's request, after a mandatory waiting period if there is hope of payment; Abu Yusuf's requirement of two installments is a minority view.

intermediate
8 min
How Many Missed Installments Trigger Ta'jiz? Abu Yusuf vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on number of missed installments for ta'jiz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn on the required number of missed installments for ta'jiz, identify their respective proofs, and apply the azhar (more apparent) view.

Core Idea
One missed installment? Two? The Hanafi school splits—Abu Yusuf requires two consecutive, the Sahibayn say one suffices.

In this passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute on the declaration of a mukatab's incapacity (ta'jiz). The disagreement centres on how many missed installments are needed before the master can have the mukatab returned to slavery. Imam Abu Yusuf argues for two consecutive missed installments, citing a hadith of Ali (RA) and the nature of kitaba as a contract of ease. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that one missed installment is enough, based on qiyas and the practice of Ibn Umar. The author weighs the evidence and declares Abu Yusuf's view the more apparent (azhar).

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the two Hanafi positions
Identify the contested ruling: after how many missed installments can a mukatab be declared 'ajiz? Note that both positions are within the Hanafi school.
2
Follow Abu Yusuf's proof
Examine Abu Yusuf's two proofs: (1) the hadith of Ali (RA) explicitly conditioning ta'jiz on two consecutive installments; (2) the kitaba contract is one of ease (irfaq), so a grace period is inherent, and the best grace period is what the parties agreed upon – the term of one installment.
The hadith is narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah and al-Bayhaqi.
3
Follow the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue by qiyas: one who cannot pay one installment is even less able to pay two, so incapacity is established with one missed installment. They also cite the practice of Ibn Umar who returned a mukatabah for missing one installment.
The Sahibayn's view is also a valid Hanafi position.
4
Examine the Hanafi response (author's resolution)
The author notes that the athar (reports from Companions) are conflicting: Ibn Umar's action may have involved two installments or a different scenario. Since the hadith of Ali is explicit and the athars conflict, Abu Yusuf's view is more apparent (azhar).
The response does not reject the Sahibayn's view outright but weighs the evidence.
5
Determine the tarjih and thamarah al-khilaf
The azhar (more apparent) view is that of Imam Abu Yusuf: two consecutive missed installments are required. The practical difference: the mukatab gets a longer grace period under Abu Yusuf's view; under the Sahibayn's view, the master can act after one missed installment.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) today follows Abu Yusuf's view as per the author's tarjih.

قوله ولانه عقد ارفاق ... الخ - يد امام ابو یوسف کی عقلی دلیل ہے کہ عقد کتابت تو عقد ارفاق ہے جو سہولت و آسانی پر معنی ہے یہاں تک کہ عمدہ ترین عقد کتابت وہی ہوتا ہے جو میعادی ہو بلکہ امام شافعی اور امام احمد کے نزدیک تو میعاد کا ہونا لازمی ہے اور ادائیگی کا وجوب قسط کی میعاد آنے پر ہوتا ۔ ہے تو بغرض آسانی کسی قدر مدت تک مہلت دینا ضروری ہے اور اولی مدت وہی ہے جس پر دونوں عاقدوں نے اتفاق کیا ہے اور وہ ایک قسط کی مدت ہے۔

قوله ولهما ان سب الخ - طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ سب منی یعنی مکاتب کا عاجز ہونا تحقیق ہو چکا اس لئے کہ جب وہ ایک قسط کی ادائیگی سے عاجز ہے تو دو قسطوں کی ادائیگی سے کہیں زیادہ عاجز ہو گا بخلاف دو تین روز کے کہ اتنی مہلت تولا بدی ہے۔ اور بجر کا سبب فسخ ہونا اس لئے ہے کہ آقا کا مقصد یہ تھا کہ قسط کا وقت آنے پر مال وصول ہو اور یہ مقصد فوت ہو گیا تو جب آقا اس کے بغیر راضی نہیں تھا تو عقد فسخ کر دیا جائے گا۔ قوله والاثار متعارضة ... الخ - امام ابو یوسف نے جو حضرت علی کے اثر مذکور سے استدلال کیا ہے۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ اس بارے میں آثار صحابہ باہم متعارض ہیں۔ چنانچہ حضرت ابن عمر سے مروی ہے کہ ان کی ایک مکاتبہ باندی ایک قسط کی ادائیگی سے عاجز ہوئی تو کتابت نسخ کر کے اس کو رقیق کر دیا۔ پس ان آثار سے احتجاج ساقط ہو گیا۔ لیکن اثر ابن عمر میں ایک قسط کی قید نہیں ہے بلک ابن ابی شیبہ نے حضرت عطاء سے روایت کیا ہے ان ابن عمر کاتب غلاماً له على الف دينار فاداها الاماته فرده فى الرق " کہ حضرت ابن عمر نے اپنے ایک غلام کو ہزار دینار پر مکاتب کیا غلام نے نوسو دینا ر ادا کر دئیے اور سود دینار نہیں دے سکا تو حضرت ابن عمرؓ نے اس کو رقیق کر دیا۔ پس ظاہر یہ ہے کہ وہ سو دینار ادا کرنے سے عاجز ہو گیا تھا یا متواتر دو قسطوں کی میعاد گذرگئی تھی۔ بہر کیف اول تو یہ حضرت عمر کا فعل ہے اور اثر علی میں قول ہے دوم یہ کہ دونوں میں کوئی معارضہ نہیں ہے۔ پس اظہر قول امام ابو یوسف کا ہے۔ کسی قسط کے خلل کا سلطان کے علاوہ کے پاس خدشہ ظاہر کیا اور عاجز ہو گیا اگر نے اس کی رضامندی سے اسے رقیق کر دے تو یہ جائز ہے لان الكتابة تفسخ بالتراضى من غير عذر قبالعند اولى ولو لم يرض به العبد لابد من القضاء بالفسخ لانه عقد لازم تام فلابد من القضاء او الرضاء كالرد بالعيب بعد القبض . قال واذا عجز المكاتب عاد الى احكام الرق لانفساخ الكتابة وما كان فى يده من الاكساب فهو لمولاه لانه ظهر انه كسب عبده وهذا لانه كان موقوفا عليه او على مولاه وقد زال التوقف
Book of the Mukatab. Abu Yusuf: He is not declared incapable until two installments become due consecutively, based on the saying of Ali: 'When two installments become due consecutively on the mukatab, he is returned to slavery.' He tied it to this condition. And because the contract of kitaba is a contract of ease, so its best form is deferred, and obligation occurs after the installment is due, so a period of grace is necessary for ease, and the best period is what the two parties agree upon. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: The cause of dissolution has occurred, which is incapacity, because one who is incapable of paying one installment is even more incapable of paying two. This is because the master's aim is to receive the money at the due date, and this has been missed, so the contract is dissolved if he is not satisfied with less. Unlike two or three days, because they are necessary for possibility of payment, so it is not delay. The athars are conflicting: it is narrated from Ibn Umar that a mukatabah of his was unable to pay one installment, so he returned her to slavery, so argument with it falls. Language explanation... Translation: If he has no means of money and the master requests to declare him incapable, the judge declares him incapable and annuls the kitaba. This is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Imam Abu Yusuf says: He should not declare him incapable until two consecutive installments become due, due to the saying of Ali: 'When two consecutive installments become due on the mukatab, he is returned to slavery.' So he tied it to this condition. And because the kitaba contract is a contract of ease, so its best form is deferred, and obligation occurs after the installment is due, so some period of grace is necessary for ease, and the best period is what both parties agree upon. The proof of the two is that the cause of dissolution, i.e., incapacity, has been realized, because one who is incapable of paying one installment is even more incapable of paying two. This is because the master's aim was to receive the money at the due date, and this aim has been missed, so the contract is dissolved when he is not satisfied without it. Unlike two or three days, because they are necessary for possibility of payment, so this is not delay. The athars of the companions are conflicting: it is narrated from Ibn Umar that a mukatabah of his failed to pay one installment, so he made her a slave. So argument with these athars falls. Commentary... His statement: 'If he has no means' etc. – If the mukatab has no means of obtaining money, and after three days he still cannot pay, and the master requests to declare him incapable, the judge rules on his incapacity and annuls the kitaba. This is according to the two. Imam Abu Yusuf says: He should not rule on incapacity until two consecutive installments become due. Imam Ahmad, Ibn Abi Layla, Sufyan bin Uyaynah, and Hasan bin Hayy also hold this view. Proof: The statement of Ali, narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah in al-Musannaf and by al-Bayhaqi in al-Sunan: 'When two consecutive installments become due on the mukatab and he does not pay them, he is returned to slavery.' So Ali tied ta'jiz to this condition, and what is conditional does not exist before the condition. This is not known by analogy... and what is narrated from him is like what is narrated from him (the Prophet). His statement: 'And because it is a contract of ease' etc. – This is Abu Yusuf's rational proof: the kitaba contract is a contract of ease, based on facility, so its best form is deferred. In fact, according to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, deferral is necessary. Obligation occurs after the installment is due, so for ease some period of grace is necessary, and the best period is what both parties agree upon, which is the period of one installment. His statement: 'And the two say: the cause' etc. – The proof of the two is that the cause, i.e., the mukatab's incapacity, has been realized, because when he is incapable of paying one installment, he is even more incapable of paying two. Unlike two or three days, which is not delay. The cause for dissolution is that the master's aim was to receive money at the due date, and this aim is lost, so when the master is not satisfied without it, the contract is dissolved. His statement: 'And the athars are conflicting' etc. – The response to Abu Yusuf's argument with the athar of Ali is that the athars of the companions are conflicting in this regard. It is narrated from Ibn Umar that a mukatabah of his failed to pay one installment, so he annulled the kitaba and made her a slave. So argument with these athars falls. However, in the athar of Ibn Umar, it is not specified as one installment; rather, Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated from Ata that Ibn Umar made a kitaba with his slave for one thousand dinars, and the slave paid nine hundred but could not pay the remaining one hundred, so Ibn Umar returned him to slavery. So apparently he was unable to pay the one hundred, or two consecutive installments had passed. In any case, first, this is the action of Ibn Umar, while the athar of Ali is a saying; second, there is no contradiction. So the more apparent view is that of Abu Yusuf. If he defaults on an installment before a non-sultan authority and becomes incapable, and the master returns him to slavery with his consent, it is permissible because kitaba is dissolved by mutual consent even without excuse, so with excuse it is more appropriate. If the slave does not consent, a court order for dissolution is necessary because it is a binding complete contract, so court order or consent is required, like return due to defect after taking possession. He said: When the mukatab becomes incapable, he returns to the rulings of slavery due to the dissolution of kitaba, and whatever earnings were in his hand belong to his master because it becomes clear that it was the earnings of his slave, and this is because it was suspended until he or his master [took it] and the suspension is removed.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya deals with an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the declaration of a mukatab's incapacity (ta'jiz) to pay his kitaba installments. The core question: after how many missed installments can the master have the mukatab declared 'ajiz and return him to slavery? The two (Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that one missed installment suffices, because if he cannot pay one, he is even less able to pay two. They argue that the master's purpose is to receive payment at the due date; when that fails, the contract should be dissolved. They also point to the practice of Ibn Umar, who returned a mukatab to slavery for missing a single installment. Imam Abu Yusuf, however, holds that two consecutive missed installments are required, citing the hadith of Ali (narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah and al-Bayhaqi) that explicitly ties the return to slavery to the imposition of two consecutive installments. He also reasons that the kitaba contract is one of ease (irfaq), so a grace period is natural; the best grace period is what the parties agreed upon—typically the term of one installment. Thus, the mukatab should be given the entire period of the current installment, and only if two successive installments lapse without payment can incapacity be declared. The text notes that the athars (reports from Companions) are conflicting, and ultimately the author leans toward Abu Yusuf's view as more apparent (azhar). The passage also discusses the procedure when the mukatab defaults before a non-sultan authority: if both parties consent to annulment, it is valid; otherwise, a judge's ruling is required because the kitaba is a binding contract (lazim tamm). Finally, when incapacity is established, the mukatab returns to full slavery, and his earnings during the kitaba period belong to the master, as the suspension of ownership is removed.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered a contract of kitaba with his master to buy his freedom by paying installments.
تعجيز (ta'jīz): Legal declaration that the mukatab is unable to pay his installments, resulting in annulment of kitaba and return to slavery.
نجم (najm): An installment of the kitaba payment due at a specified time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf, after how many consecutive missed installments can a mukatab be declared incapable?
A) One installment
B) Two consecutive installments
C) Three consecutive installments
D) Any missed installment immediately

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's azhar view requires two consecutive missed installments before a mukatab can be declared incapable, balancing the master's right with the mukatab's need for ease.

Missing a Payment in Mukatabah: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad al-Shaybani
Discusses the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on dissolution due to missed payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether missing a payment in a kitabah contract automatically dissolves it and identify the preferred view.

Core Idea
The kitabah contract is binding; it does not dissolve automatically upon missed payment — mutual consent or court order is required.

In the Hanafi school, the kitabah contract (contract of manumission by installments) is a binding agreement that grants the slave certain rights. A key internal dispute arises when a mukatab (the slave under kitabah) fails to pay an installment: does that automatically dissolve the contract? This passage from al-Hidayah presents the arguments of two leading Hanafi authorities — Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani — and resolves the conflict by preferring Abu Yusuf's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the issue
State who is disputing (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and what they disagree about: whether missing a payment automatically dissolves the kitabah contract.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between madhahib.
2
State Abu Yusuf's argument
Abu Yusuf argues that kitabah is a contract of leniency (عقد ارفاق), so a grace period is needed. Missing one payment does not imply total incapacity.
He also cites an athar from Ali (رضي الله عنه).
3
State Muhammad al-Shaybani's argument
Muhammad argues that missing one payment reveals the mukatab's incapacity, so the contract dissolves automatically. He cites the hadith 'سب مني' and an athar from Ibn Umar.
4
Examine the conflicting athar and the preferred view
The athar are conflicting; the text prefers the athar from Ali (رضي الله عنه) which supports Abu Yusuf. Thus, the mufta bihi position is that dissolution requires mutual consent or a court order.
The contract is binding (lāzim), so it cannot be dissolved unilaterally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ولانه عقد ارفاق ... الخ
قوله ولهما ان سب الخ
قوله والاثار متعارضة ... الخ
فان اخل بنجم عند غير السلطان فعجز فرده مولاه برضاه فهو جائز لان الكتابة تفسخ بالتراضى من غير عذر فبالعذر اولى ولو لم يرض به العبد لابد من القضاء بالفسخ لانه عقد لازم تام فلابد من القضاء او الرضاء كالرد بالعيب بعد القبض . قال واذا عجز المكاتب عاد الى احكام الرق لانفساخ الكتابة وما كان فى يده من الاكساب فهو لمولاه لانه ظهر انه كسب عبده وهذا لانه كان موقوفا عليه او على مولاه وقد زال التوقف
His saying: 'And because it is a contract of leniency'... His saying: 'And for them both, that سب'... His saying: 'And the athar are conflicting'... If he fails to pay an installment in front of other than the sultan and becomes incapable, then his master made him a slave with his consent, it is permissible because the kitabah contract is dissolved by mutual consent even without an excuse, so with an excuse it is more appropriate. And if the slave does not consent, court dissolution is necessary because it is a binding and complete contract, so court or consent is necessary, like the return due to a defect after possession. He said: And if the mukatab becomes incapable, he returns to the rulings of slavery due to the dissolution of the kitabah, and whatever earnings are in his possession belong to his master, because it appears that it is his slave's earnings, and this is because it was suspended between him and his master, and the suspension has ended.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute followed by a ruling. It begins by presenting the rational proof of Imam Abu Yusuf: the kitabah contract is one of leniency, so some grace period is essential. His saying 'قوله ولهما' refers to the proof of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the two) that if a mukatab fails to pay one installment, he is incapable of paying the rest, so the contract dissolves. Then comes the response: the athar (traditions) of the Companions are conflicting, with one from Ali supporting Abu Yusuf and one from Ibn Umar opposing. The preferred view is that of Abu Yusuf. The Arabic text then states the ruling: if the mukatab misses a payment in front of someone other than a judge (sultan) and the master enslaves him with his consent, it is valid. If the slave does not consent, a court order is required because the contract is binding and complete, analogous to returning a defective item after possession. The passage concludes that if the mukatab becomes unable to pay, the contract dissolves, he returns to slavery, and his earnings belong to the master.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
کتابت (kitābah): A contract between a master and a slave whereby the slave is promised freedom upon payment of a specified sum.
نجم (najm): An installment payment in the kitabah contract, usually due at predetermined times.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the passage, what is required if a mukatab misses a payment and the master wants to end the contract?
A) The contract dissolves automatically.
B) The master must obtain the mukatab's consent or a court order.
C) The master can immediately reclaim the slave.
D) The master must forgive the debt.

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi view is that a kitabah contract is binding and does not dissolve automatically upon non-payment; dissolution requires mutual consent or a court order.

Ruling on the Death of a Mukatab with Property
Ruling on the death of a mukatab with sufficient property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a mukatab dies leaving property sufficient to pay the kitabah debt and explain the evidence from the Companions.

Core Idea
The kitabah does not dissolve at death; the mukatab is freed retroactively, and his debt is paid from his estate.

In this lesson, we examine a unique situation: a slave under a kitabah contract (mukatab) dies before completing payment but leaves enough property to cover the debt. How does the Hanafi school treat the contract, the slave's freedom, and his children's status? The answer illustrates the Hanafi approach to honoring contracts and the retroactive effect of emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
The act is the death of a mukatab who owns property sufficient to pay the remaining kitabah debt. Identify the legal consequence stated in the passage.
2
Determine the ruling on the kitabah contract
The passage explicitly states that the kitabah does not dissolve. Understand the implication: the contract remains valid and enforceable after death.
3
Analyze the consequences
List the four consequences mentioned: debt payment, freedom decree at last moment, inheritance of remainder, and children's freedom. Note the retroactive nature of freedom.
4
Locate the evidence (dalil)
The passage cites the saying of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud. Recognize that in Hanafi usul, the statement of a Companion (qawl sahabi) is a valid proof when it does not contradict qiyas.
5
Understand the authority weight
The text says 'wa bihi akhadha 'ulama'una' — our scholars have adopted this. This indicates that the ruling is from the relied-upon positions (zahir al-riwayah) in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال فان مات المكاتب وله مال لم ينفسخ الكتابة وقضى ما عليه من ماله وحكم بعتقه في آخر جزء من اجزاء حياته وما بقى فهو ميراث لورثته ويعتق اولاده وهذا قول على وابن مسعود و به اخذ علمائنا
He said: If the mukatab dies and he has property, the kitabah does not dissolve, and what he owes is paid from his property, and his freedom is decreed in the last part of his life, and what remains is inheritance for his heirs, and his children become free. This is the saying of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, and our scholars have adopted it.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil from the Companions). It addresses the situation where a mukatab dies leaving enough property to cover the remaining kitabah debt. The ruling states that the kitabah contract does not dissolve upon his death. Instead, the outstanding amount is paid from his estate, and he is considered a free man from the last moment of his life. Consequently, his children become free as well. Any remaining property after paying the debt is distributed among his legal heirs. The ruling is traced to Ali (رضي الله عنه) and Ibn Mas'ud (رضي الله عنه), and the Hanafi scholars adopt this position. The commentary clarifies that this is the view of the majority of the Hanafi school, including Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukātab): A slave under a kitabah contract who dies before completing payment.
عتق (ʿitq): Emancipation; the mukatab is deemed freed at the last moment of life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The contract remains, the debt is paid from his estate, and he is deemed freed.
C) The contract dissolves, and the property is divided equally between master and heirs.
D) The contract becomes void, and the mukatab's children remain slaves.

Key Takeaway
The kitabah contract remains valid after the mukatab's death if his estate can pay the debt; he is freed retroactively, benefiting his children and heirs.

Death of a Mukatab with Property: Contract Not Void, He is Declared Free
Hanafi ruling on mukatab's death with property, companion evidence, and response to Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mukatab who dies leaving property sufficient to pay the kitabah, explain why the contract is not void, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A mukatab's kitabah contract does not void upon death—his estate pays the debt and he dies free.

This lesson addresses a critical disagreement between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the fate of a mukatab (a slave under contract for freedom) who dies before completing payment. Does the contract automatically become void, leaving him a slave? Or does his estate settle the debt, allowing him to be declared free in the last moment of his life? The passage presents the Hanafi position, supported by the practice of Imam Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, and refutes the Shafi'i argument based on the nature of the contract as a commutative transaction.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core Hanafi ruling: the kitabah contract is not voided; the remaining payment is taken from the estate, the mukatab is declared free in the last part of his life, his children are free, and the remainder goes to his heirs.
This is based on the statements of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the contract is voided, the mukatab dies a slave, and his property belongs to his master. His main evidence is the opinion of Zayd ibn Thabit.
Shafi'i also argues on rational grounds that the purpose of kitabah (freedom) cannot be achieved after death.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Analyze Shafi'i's rational argument: he considers three possibilities for establishing freedom after death and finds each impossible. Understand his reasoning.
The three possibilities: freedom established after death (no capacity), before death (payment not made), or after death with retroactive effect (impossible in his view).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond by analogizing kitabah to a commutative contract (mu'awadah) like sale, which is not voided by the death of either party. They also argue that the mukatab's right is stronger because the contract is binding on the master.
Freedom can be established retrospectively by deeming the payment by the estate as the mukatab's own payment.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference between the two schools: in the Hanafi school, the mukatab dies free, his children are free, and his heirs inherit the surplus; in the Shafi'i school, he dies a slave, his children remain slaves, and the master takes his property.
This highlights the real-world impact of the legal disagreement.

قال كان زيد بن ثابت يقول : المكاتب عبد مابقی علیه درهم لا يرث ولا يورث وكان على. يقول: اذا مات المكاتب وترك مالاً قسم ماترك على ما ادى وعلى ما بقى فما اصاب ما ادى فللورثة وما اصاب مابقى فلمو اليه وكان عبا الله يقول يؤدى الى مواليه مابقى من مكاتبته ولورثته مابقى

وقال الشافعي تبطل الكتابة ويموت عبدا وما ترك لمولاه و امامه فى ذلك زيد بن ثابت ولان المقصود من الكتابة عتقه وقد تعذر اثباته فتبطل وهذا لانه لا يخلو اما ان يثبت بعد الممات مقصوراً او يثبت قبله او بعده مستندا لا وجه الى الاول لعدم المحلية ولا الى الثانى لفقد الشرط وهو الاداء ولا الى الثالث لتعذر الثبوت فى الحال والشيء يثبت ثم يستند ولنا انه عقد معاوضة ولا يبطل بموت احد المتعاقدين وهو المولى فكذا بموت الآخر والجامع بينهما الحاجة الى ابقاء العقد لاحياء الحق بل اولى لان حقه أكد من حق المولى حتى لزم العقد في جانبه والموت الفى للمالكية منه للمملوكية فينزل حيا تقديرا أو يستند الحرية باستناد سبب الاداء الى ما قبل الموت ويكون اداء خلفه کادائه وكل ذلك ممكن على ما عرف تمامه في الخلافيات
He said: If the mukatab dies and he has property, the kitabah is not voided, and what is due from him is paid from his property, and he is judged to be free in the last part of his life, and what remains is inherited by his heirs, and his children are free. This is the statement of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, and our scholars have adopted it. Commentary: If the mukatab dies leaving enough estate to pay the kitabah, according to us the kitabah contract is not voided; rather, the kitabah is paid from his estate, he is declared free in the last part of his life, and whatever remains goes to his heirs. This is also the view of Ata, Tawus, Hasan al-Basri, Hasan ibn Hayy, Masid, Ibn Sirin, Shaqiq al-Shami, Amr ibn Dinar, Sufyan al-Thawri, and Ibn Rahawayh. Also the view of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, as narrated by al-Bayhaqi. Zayd ibn Thabit said: The mukatab remains a slave as long as a dirham remains unpaid on him; he neither inherits nor is inherited from. Ali said: When the mukatab dies leaving property, his estate is divided based on what he paid and what remains; the share corresponding to what he paid goes to his heirs, and the share corresponding to what remains goes to his master. Abdullah ibn Mas'ud said: The remainder of his kitabah is paid to his master, and what remains is for his heirs. Also in the Musannaf of Abd al-Razzaq from Sufyan al-Thawri: Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr wrote to Ali asking about two Muslims who became apostates, a Muslim who committed zina with a Christian woman, and a mukatab who died leaving a remainder of his kitabah and free children. Ali wrote: As for the two who apostatized, if they repent, well; otherwise strike their necks. As for the Muslim, establish the hadd punishment upon him and hand the Christian woman over to her people. As for the mukatab, pay the remainder of his kitabah, and whatever remains is for his free children. Imam al-Shafi'i said: The kitabah is voided, he dies a slave, and whatever he leaves belongs to his master. His evidence is Zayd ibn Thabit. Also because the purpose of kitabah is his freedom, and establishing it has become impossible, so it is voided. This is because either freedom is established after death in a limited sense, or before death, or after death with retroactive effect. The first is impossible due to lack of capacity, the second due to absence of the condition (payment), and the third due to impossibility of establishing it in the present, whereas a thing is established and then retroactive. Our response: It is a commutative contract, and it is not voided by the death of one of the contracting parties, namely the master; similarly by the death of the other. The common link is the need to preserve the contract to keep the right alive. Rather, it is more so because the right of the mukatab is stronger than the right of the master, as the contract is binding from his side. And death removes ownership from him and he reverts to being a slave, so he is treated as if alive constructively, or freedom is established with retroactive effect by connecting the cause of payment to before death, and the payment by his successor is like his own payment. All of this is possible as is known in detail in works on ikhtilaf.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the death of a mukatab (a slave under a kitabah contract) who leaves property. The Hanafi school, relying on the sayings of Imam Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, rules that the kitabah contract does not become void. Instead, the remaining payment is taken from his estate, he is declared free in the last part of his life, his children are free, and whatever remains of the estate goes to his heirs. The commentary mentions that many early scholars such as Ata, Tawus, Hasan al-Basri, and others held this view. Imam al-Shafi'i, following Zayd ibn Thabit, argues that the contract becomes void because the purpose of kitabah is freedom, which cannot be realized after death. He analyzes three scenarios for establishing freedom after death, all of which he finds impossible. The Hanafi response is that kitabah is a commutative contract (mu'awadah) analogous to a sale, and just as the death of one party does not void the contract, similarly the death of the mukatab does not void it. In fact, the mukatab's right is stronger because the contract is binding on the master. Moreover, freedom can be established retrospectively by considering the payment as having been made, thus he is deemed free in the last moment of his life.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has a contract (kitabah) with his master to earn his freedom by paying a specified sum.
الكتابة (al-kitabah): The contract between a master and a slave whereby the slave pays a sum in installments or lump sum in exchange for eventual freedom.
فسخ (faskh): The dissolution or voiding of a contract; the passage discusses whether the kitabah contract is dissolved upon the mukatab's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens when a mukatab dies leaving property sufficient to pay the kitabah?
A) He dies a slave and his property goes to his master.
B) The contract is void, his property goes to his master.
C) He is declared free, his estate pays the kitabah, and the remainder goes to his heirs.
D) His children remain slaves.

Key Takeaway
The kitabah contract is not voided by the mukatab's death if he leaves property; his estate pays the debt, and he dies free.

advanced
15 min
Death of the Mukatab Does Not Void the Kitabah Contract
Inter-madhhab dispute on kitabah contract validity after mukatab's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of the kitabah contract after the mukatab's death, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument with its evidence, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between the two schools.

Core Idea
A kitabah contract survives the mukatab's death as a compensatory contract, with freedom achieved posthumously via estate payment.

This lesson examines a key disagreement between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the fate of a kitabah (manumission contract) when the slave (mukatab) dies before completing payment. The Hanafi school argues that the contract is not voided; instead, the mukatab's estate continues paying the price, and freedom is recognized retroactively. Understanding this dispute reveals the school's methodology in using qiyas and its prioritization of the slave's right to freedom.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What does the Hanafi school rule regarding the kitabah contract after the mukatab's death? State it concisely.
The contract remains valid; freedom is achieved posthumously through payment from the estate.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which scholars oppose the Hanafi view and what exactly do they hold?
Imam al-Shafi'i, Abu Qatada, Sulayman, Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, and Imam Ahmad hold the contract becomes void and the mukatab dies a slave.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What evidence (naqli and aqli) does the Shafi'i school present? List both the athar and the rational argument.
Naqli: Zaid ibn Thabit's statement that a mukatab remains a slave as long as a dirham is owed. Aqli: freedom cannot be established after death due to lack of locus, unfulfilled condition, and impossibility of attribution.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the opponent's proof? Identify the qiyas used and the legal fictions proposed.
Qiyas to marriage (compensatory contract surviving death). Response to impossibility: consider mukatab alive for this purpose (as in debt cases) or attribute freedom to the moment before death with payment by deputy.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the two positions in a real case?
Hanafi: estate pays kitabah price, freedom established, heirs inherit. Shafi'i: contract void, estate goes to master, no freedom.

خود ادا کرنے کے ہوگا ۔ فلا يلزم تقديم المشروط سوال . اداء فعل حسی ہے اور استناد تصرفات شرعیہ میں ہوتا ہے۔
Chapter of the Mukatab. 19. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary of Hidaya volume 12. Regarding their leader Hadhrat Zaid bin Thabit, and because the purpose of kitabah is his freedom, and establishing it became difficult, therefore the kitabah becomes void. The reason is that freedom is not free from either being established after death as restricted, or established before death, or established after death as attributed. The first has no reason due to lack of capacity, the second also has no reason due to the condition (payment) being absent, and the third also has no reason due to current impossibility of establishment, while a thing is first established then attributed. Our proof is that kitabah is a compensatory contract and it does not become void by the death of one of the contractors, i.e., the master. So likewise it will not become void by the death of the other. And the common factor between them is the need to preserve the contract for the fulfillment of rights. Rather, it will be preserved with more priority, because the slave's right is more confirmed than the master's, to the extent that from his side this contract is binding, and death negates ownership more than it negates being owned. So the slave will be considered alive, or due to the attribution of the cause of payment, freedom will also be attributed to a time before death, and his deputy's payment will be like his own payment, and all this is possible, as its full discussion is well-known in the polemics. Commentary: He said, 'And al-Shafi'i said...' Al-Imam al-Shafi'i says that the kitabah contract will be rescinded, and the mukatab will depart this world in a state of slavery, and his estate will go to the master. Abu Qatada, Sulayman, Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, and Imam Ahmad also held this view. The proof is the statement of Hadhrat Zaid ibn Thabit: 'The mukatab is a slave as long as a dirham remains, he does not inherit nor is he inherited.' Al-Bayhaqi after verifying it said that Ibn Umar and Aisha also had this saying, and there is also a narration from Umar. He said, 'And because the purpose...' This is the rational proof of Imam al-Shafi'i: the purpose of kitabah was that the mukatab become free, and due to his death, establishing freedom became impossible, therefore the kitabah becomes void. The clarification of the impossibility of the right is that if freedom is established, it is not free from three states: 1. Its establishment after death as restricted (i.e., not established earlier and not attributed to any time). 2. Established before death. 3. Established after death by attribution to a time (e.g., after his death it is ruled that he became free at such and such time of his life). All three are invalid: 1. Because after death he is no longer a locus for freedom (since a right requires a locus). 2. Because freedom is conditioned upon payment, and the condition (payment) is absent. 3. Because a thing is established first then attributed to a time, while here freedom cannot be established at first. Since the three possibilities are impossible, there is no way for the mukatab to become free, so he died a slave. He said, 'And we argue that it is a contract...' Our proof is that the kitabah contract is a compensatory contract (not in the sense of marriage and agency, etc., because for the mukatab ownership of earning and for the master the claim of the kitabah price is established, and compensation requires equality). So just as the kitabah contract does not become void by the death of one of the contractors, i.e., the master, but remains by consensus, similarly it will not become void by the death of the mukatab. Because the common cause is the need to fulfill the right: after the master's death, the contract is kept to achieve his purpose, i.e., the wala' (patronage); likewise after the mukatab's death, the contract will be kept so that he may attain the honor of freedom. He said, 'Rather, more so...' Rather, in the case of the mukatab's death, the kitabah contract will be preserved with more priority, because the mukatab's right is stronger than the master's. This is because from the mukatab's side the contract is binding, such that if the master wants to cancel it, he cannot. In contrast, the master's right to the kitabah price is not binding, such that if the mukatab incapacitates himself, the master's right to the payment becomes void. So if the master's right, which is not strongly established, does not become void upon his death, then the mukatab's right, which is more strongly established, will a fortiori not become void upon his death. He said, 'And death negates...' And death negates ownership more than it negates being owned, because being owned means being under control and subjugation, while ownership means control and domination. Therefore an inanimate object can be described as being owned but not as owning, and death negates control and domination more than it negates being under control and subjugation. So when with the stronger of the two opposites (ownership) it is permissible to preserve the contract due to the lesser of the two needs, then with the weaker opposite (being owned) it is a fortiori permissible to preserve the contract due to the greater need. In other words: when the contract was preserved due to the master's death, even though there is no ownership (since the master is dead), then a fortiori it will be preserved due to the mukatab's death, since here there is no being owned. He said, 'So he is considered alive...' This is a two-fold answer to the rational doubt raised by Imam al-Shafi'i, each adopted by a group of our scholars. The explanation is that the establishment of the mukatab's freedom is possible in either way: because in the case of freedom being established after death, the slave will be considered alive, just as when a deceased person's debt exceeds the estate, the estate is considered his property by considering him alive. Similarly, for the purpose of shrouding and burial, and for executing bequests from one-third of the property, he is considered alive. He said, 'Or attributed...' Or it will be ruled that his freedom occurred in the last part of his life before death, and the cause of freedom, which is payment, is attributed to a time before death, so freedom is also attributed to a time before death. If someone says that this would necessitate the precedence of the conditioned over the condition, we respond that the deputy's payment is equivalent to his own payment, so the precedence of the conditioned does not arise. Payment is a physical act, while attribution occurs in legal dispositions.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the validity of the kitabah contract upon the death of the mukatab (a slave who has contracted to earn his freedom by paying installments). The commentator (Ashraf al-Hidaya) first presents the Shafi'i position: the contract is rescinded, the mukatab dies a slave, and his estate goes to his master. He supports this with a statement of Zaid ibn Thabit (a companion) that the mukatab remains a slave so long as a dirham is owed; he neither inherits nor is inherited. He also provides a rational argument: the purpose of kitabah is freedom, which cannot be established after death because the mukatab is no longer a locus, the condition of payment is not fulfilled, and freedom cannot be attributed posthumously. The Hanafi school responds by arguing that kitabah is a compensatory contract (mu'awadah), analogous to marriage, which does not dissolve upon the death of either party. They claim that the mukatab's right to freedom is stronger than the master's right to payment; if the master's death does not void the contract, the mukatab's death a fortiori should not. The commentator provides a detailed rational argument: death negates ownership more than it negates being owned, so if the contract can survive the death of the owner (who loses property), it can more easily survive the death of the owned (who loses nothing). He then answers the Shafi'i impossibility argument by proposing two possibilities: either the mukatab is considered alive for legal purposes (as in the case of debts and shroud), or his freedom is attributed to a moment just before death, with payment performed by his deputy (the executor of his estate). Thus the contract remains valid and freedom is achieved posthumously.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract (kitabah) with his master to earn and pay a specified sum in installments in exchange for freedom.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract of manumission by payment, where a slave agrees to pay a sum to the master in installments, upon which he becomes free.
عقد معاوضة (aqd mu'awadah): A compensatory contract where each party gives something in exchange for receiving something (e.g., sale, marriage, kitabah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to the kitabah contract when a mukatab dies before completing payment?
A) The contract is void and the mukatab dies a slave.
B) The contract is void, but the mukatab is considered free due to his effort.
C) The contract remains valid; freedom is achieved posthumously through the payment of his estate.
D) The contract is transferred to his heirs who must complete payment.

Key Takeaway
The kitabah contract is not voided by the mukatab's death in Hanafi fiqh; it remains valid as a compensatory contract, with freedom achieved posthumously through estate payment.

Kitabah After Death: Hanafi Proofs and Shafi'i Objection
Hanafi proof for kitabah validity after mukatab's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof that the kitabah contract remains valid after the death of the mukatab and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Kitabah is a mu'awada contract; the mukatab's right to freedom is more emphatic than the master's right to payment, so it survives death a fortiori.

In this lesson, we dissect the Hanafi school's rigorous defense of the kitabah contract's continuity after the mukatab's death. The commentary from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents a multi-layered argument: first, analogizing kitabah to other exchange contracts; second, using an a fortiori (awla) reasoning based on the binding nature of the mukatab's rights; third, a subtle principle about death's effect on ownership versus being owned; and finally, two legal fictions to counter the objection that a dead person cannot acquire freedom. This lesson walks through each layer, showing how the Hanafi method combines qiyas, ihtiyat, and legal attribution (isnad).

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
Does the kitabah contract become void when the mukatab dies? The Hanafi school says no; Imam al-Shafi'i says yes.
The dispute centers on whether a dead person can be granted freedom through contract.
2
Examine the first Hanafi proof
Kitabah is a mu'awada contract. Just as the master's death does not void the contract (by consensus), the mukatab's death should not void it either, because the need to revive rights is common.
The common effective cause is the need to achieve the object of the contract: wala' for the master, freedom for the mukatab.
3
Analyze the a fortiori refinement
The mukatab's right is more emphatic because the contract is binding on the master (he cannot annul it) but not on the mukatab (he can render himself incapable). Thus, if the weaker right survives the master's death, the stronger right should a fortiori survive the mukatab's death.
This uses the principle of awla (greater priority).
4
Examine the second proof
Death negates ownership more than being owned. Since the contract was retained after the master's death despite absence of ownership, it should be retained a fortiori after the mukatab's death where only being owned is absent.
This argument inverts the difficulty: if it works when the negation is stronger, it works when the negation is weaker.
5
Analyze the two responses to Shafi'i
Shafi'i objects that a dead person cannot own or be owned. Hanafis respond: (1) treat the dead mukatab as alive in certain rights (like debts or bequests), or (2) attribute the freedom to the last moment of life via legal attribution (isnad) of the cause (payment) to that time.
The archer example illustrates isnad: if an archer dies before his arrow hits, the game still becomes his because the cause was valid during life.

قوله والموت انفي.... الخ - اور موت مملوکیت کی بہ نسبت مالکیت کی زیادہ نفی کرنے والی ہے کیونکہ ملوکیت مقدور بیت و مقہوریت سے عبارت ہے اور مالکیت ، قادریت و قاہریت سے عبارت ہے۔ اس لئے جماد مملوک ہونے کے ساتھ متصف ہو سکتا ہے مالک ہونے کے ساتھ متصف نہیں ہو سکتا اور موت مقدوریت و مقہوریت کی بہ نسبت قادریت و قاہر یت کی زیادہ نفی کرنے والی ہے۔ پس جب اقوی المتنافیین کے ہوتے ہوئے ادنی الحاجتین کے پیش نظر عقد کو باقی رکھنا جائز ٹھہرا تو ادنى المتنافیین کے ساتھ اعلی الحاجتین کے پیش نظر عقد کو باقی رکھنا بطریق اولی جائز ہوگا ۔ بالفاظ دیگر یوں کہو کہ جب موت آقا کی وجہ سے عقد باقی رکھا گیا۔ حالانکہ اس میں ملکیت ندارد ہے تو موت مکاتب کی وجہ سے بدرجہ اولی باقی رکھا جائے گا کہ یہاں مملوکیت ندارد ہے۔ قوله فينزل حياً .... الخ امام شافعی نے جو عقلی دلیل میں تردید ذکر کی تھی یہ اس کا دو طریق سے جواب ہے جن میں سے ہر ایک کو ہمارے اصحاب کی ایک جماعت نے اختیار کیا ہے۔ جواب کی تشریح یہ ہے کہ مکاتب کی آزادی کا ثبوت بہر طریق ممکن ہے اس لئے کہ موت کے بعد آزادی ثابت ہونے کی صورت میں غلام کو زندہ فرض کیا جائے گا۔ جیسے اگر میت کے ذمہ دین مستغرق ہو تو ترکہ کو اس کی ملک پر باقی رکھنے کے حق میں زندہ فرض کیا جاتا ہے۔ اسی طرح تجہیز و تکفین کے حق میں اور تہائی مال سے تنفیذ وصیت کے حق میں بھی زندہ فرض کیا جاتا ہے۔

خود ادا کرنے کے ہوگا ۔ فلا يلزم تقديم المشروط سوال . اداء فعل حسی ہے اور استناد تصرفات شرعیہ میں ہوتا ہے۔
جواب ٹھیک ہے لیکن نائب کا فعل منوب کی طرف مضاف ہوتاہے اور یہ اضافت شرعیہ ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر کوئی شخص شکار پر تیر چلائے اور تیر لگنے سے پہلے تیرانداز کا انتقال ہو جائے اس کے بعد شکار کے تیر لگے تو وہ شکار اسی کی ملک ہوتا ہے۔ حالانکہ میت لائق ملکیت نہیں ہے۔
مگر چونکہ اس کا سبب صحیح ہو چکا اس لئے ملک اس کی حیات کے آخری لمحات کی طرف مستند ہو کر ثابت ہوگئی ایسے ہی یہاں ہے۔
Kitab al-Mukatab. Ashraf al-Hidayah sharh Urdu Hidayah volume 12. His statement: 'And for us it is that it is a contract...' - Our proof is that the contract of kitabah is an exchange contract (it is not in the sense of marriage or agency etc., because for the mukatab, ownership of possession and for the master, the claim of the kitabah payment have been established, and the requirement of exchange is equality). So just as the contract of kitabah does not become void by the death of one of the two contracting parties, i.e., the master, but rather remains by agreement, similarly by the death of the mukatab it should also not become void. This is because the common effective cause is the need to revive the right: that after the death of the master, retention of the contract is necessary so that his objective may be achieved, i.e., wala' may be obtained; similarly after the death of the mukatab, the contract will be retained so that he may attain the honor of freedom. His statement: 'Nay, rather it is more appropriate...' - Rather, in the case of the death of the mukatab, the contract of kitabah will be retained with greater priority because the right of the mukatab is more emphatic relative to the right of the master. This is because on the part of the mukatab, this contract becomes binding, such that if the master wishes to annul it, he cannot do so. In contrast, the master's entitlement to the kitabah payment is not binding, such that if the mukatab renders himself incapable, the master's right to the payment becomes void. So when the master's right, being non-emphatic, does not become void by his death, then the mukatab's right, being more emphatic, should a fortiori not become void by his death. His statement: 'And death is more negating...' - And death is more negating of ownership than of being owned, because being owned (mamlukiyyah) consists of being under power and subjugation, while ownership (milkiyyah) consists of power and dominance. Therefore an inanimate object can be described as being owned but cannot be described as owning. And death negates power and dominance more than it negates being under power and subjugation. Thus when with the stronger of the two mutually exclusive states (malikiyyah vs. mamlukiyyah), considering the lesser need, it is permissible to retain the contract, then with the weaker of the two mutual exclusive states, considering the greater need, retaining the contract is a fortiori permissible. In other words: When the contract was retained due to the death of the master, despite the absence of ownership (malikiyyah) there, then due to the death of the mukatab it should be retained with greater priority, because here the absence of being owned (mamlukiyyah) is present. His statement: 'Then he is treated as alive...' - Imam al-Shafi'i had mentioned a refutation based on rational proof. This is a response to it in two ways, each of which has been adopted by a group of our companions. The explanation of the response is that the establishment of the mukatab's freedom is possible in every way, because if freedom is established after death, then the slave will be treated as alive. For example, if the deceased is burdened with debts consuming the entire estate, the estate is kept under his ownership in the right of preserving it, and he is treated as alive; similarly in the right of enshrouding and burial, and in the right of executing bequests up to one-third of the wealth, he is treated as alive. His statement: 'Or it is attributed...' - Or before death, in the last part of his life, his freedom will be judged, and the cause of freedom, which is the payment of wealth, since it is attributed to the time before death, so freedom too will be attributed to the time before death. Someone might say: In this case, the condition (payment) must precede the conditioned (freedom), but they answer it: that the payment by the mukatab's deputy is in the same position as payment by himself, so it does not necessitate the precedence of the condition. He said: 'Payment is a physical act, and attribution (isnad) occurs in legal acts.' The answer is correct, but the deputy's act is attributed to the principal, and this attribution is legal. For example, if a person shoots an arrow at game and before the arrow hits, the archer dies, and after that the arrow hits the game, then that game becomes his property, even though the dead person is not eligible for ownership. But because its cause was valid, ownership is established by being attributed to the last moments of his life. The same applies here.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The commentator explains the Hanafi proof that the kitabah contract remains valid after the death of the mukatab, responding to Imam al-Shafi'i who argued that a dead person cannot be owned or own. The first argument establishes kitabah as an exchange contract (mu'awada), which by its nature requires balance; just as the master's death does not void the contract (by consensus), the mukatab's death should not void it either. The common effective cause is the need to revive the rights: the master seeks wala', the mukatab seeks freedom. Furthermore, the mukatab's right is more emphatic because the contract is binding on the master but not on the mukatab (the mukatab can render himself incapable). Thus if the weaker right survives the master's death, the stronger right should a fortiori survive the mukatab's death. A second argument using the principle of 'death negates ownership more than being owned' shows that retaining the contract after the master's death (where ownership is absent) implies retaining it after the mukatab's death (where being owned is absent) with greater priority. Finally, two solutions are given for Shafi'i's objection that a dead person cannot acquire freedom: (1) treat the dead mukatab as alive, analogous to a deceased debtor or for funeral expenses; (2) attribute the freedom to the last moment of life before death, analogous to a hunter who dies before his arrow hits but the game becomes his property because the cause was valid. The archer example concretizes the legal principle of attribution (isnad) of effects to causes that were valid during life.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitabah contract with his master, agreeing to pay a sum in installments in exchange for eventual freedom.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract of manumission by payment; the slave agrees to pay a fixed amount to the master in installments, and upon completion, he becomes free.
عقد معاوضة (aqd mu'awada): An exchange contract where one party gives something in return for something from the other, involving mutual consideration.
استناد (isnad): Legal attribution: the effect of a valid cause is attributed to the time when the cause occurred, even if the effect materializes later.
تنزيل الميت منزلة الحي (tanzil al-mayyit manzil al-hayy): Treating a dead person as if alive for certain legal purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, why does the kitabah contract not become void upon the death of the mukatab?
A) Because the contract is a mere promise that becomes void upon death
B) Because it is an exchange contract and the mukatab's right to freedom is stronger than the master's right to payment
C) Because the master can always annul the contract after the mukatab's death
D) Because the mukatab's freedom is automatically granted at death regardless of payment

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that the kitabah contract survives the death of the mukatab because it is a binding exchange contract, and the mukatab's right to freedom is more emphatic than the master's right to payment.

Mukatab Dies Leaving a Child Born During Kitabah
Ruling on a mukatab dying with a child born during kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a mukatab dies leaving a child born during kitabah, and explain why the child is obligated to work.

Core Idea
A child born during kitabah is included in the father's contract and must work to free both.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi muamalat: when a mukatab (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom) dies without sufficient property, but leaves a child who was born during the kitabah period. The ruling in Al-Hidayah establishes that such a child is obligated to work (sa'ayah) to pay off the father's remaining installments. This demonstrates the principle of inclusion (dakhil) in the kitabah contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The mukatab dies without leaving enough property to pay off his kitabah installments.
The child must be born during the kitabah period, not before or after.
2
Identify the heir and his status
The child left is born during the kitabah contract. Hanafi law considers him part of the father's kitabah.
The child is not an ordinary heir; he is included in the contract.
3
Apply the ruling: the child must work
The child must engage in sa'ayah (work and earn) to pay off the father's remaining kitabah installments.
The child's earnings are treated as the father's earnings.
4
Determine the consequence of full payment
When the child pays all installments, the father is declared free retroactively from before his death, and the child also becomes free.
It is as if the father left sufficient property.
5
Understand the underlying principle
The child is included in the father's kitabah contract, and his earnings are like the father's, so he stands in the father's place for payment.
This principle ensures the contract's completion despite the father's death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر مال نہیں چھوڑا ہاں کتابت میں پیدا شدہ فرزند چھوڑا تو وہ اپنے باپ کی کتابت میں سعایت کرے گا
قال وان لم يترك وفاء وترك ولدا مولودا فى الكتابة سعى فى كتابة ابيه على نجومه فاذا ادى حكمنا بعتق ابيه قبل موته وعتق الولد لان الولد داخل فى كتابته و کسبه ککسبه فيخلفه في الاداء وصار كما اذا ترک وفاء
ترجمہ اور اگر اس نے اتنا کافی مال نہیں چھوڑا ہاں کتابت میں پیدا شدہ فرزند چھوڑا تو وہ اپنے باپ کی کتابت میں سعایت کرے گا اس کی اقساط پر ، جب وہ ادا کر دے تو ہم حکم دیں گے اس کے باپ کی آزادی کا اس کی موت سے پہلے اور فرزند بھی آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ فرزند اس کی کتابت میں داخل ہے اور فرزند کی کمائی بمنزلہ اس کی کمائی کے ہے تو وہ اس کی ادائیگی میں اپنے باپ کا خلیفہ ہوگا اور ایسا ہو گیا گویا اس نے مال چھوڑا ہے۔
تشریح قوله وان لم يترك الخ اور اگر مکاتب مذکور نے استامال نہیں چھوڑا جو اداء بدل کتابت کے لئے کافی ہو سکے البتہ ایک ایسا فرزند چھوڑا جو کتابت کی حالت میں پیدا ہوا تھا۔ تو وہ اپنے باپ کی کتابت میں اس کی اقساط کے مطابق سعایت کرے گا اور اداءِ بدل کے بعد حکم دے دیا جائے گا کہ اس کا باپ اپنی موت سے پہلے آزاد ہو گیا تھا اور اس کا فرزند بھی آزاد ہو جائے گا کیونکہ وہ اس کی کتابت میں داخل ہے۔
If he did not leave wealth, but left a child born during the kitabah, then he will work for the kitabah of his father according to its installments. When he pays, we will judge the freedom of his father before his death, and the child will also be free, because the child is included in his kitabah, and the child's earnings are like his earnings, so he stands in his place in payment, and it becomes as if he left wealth. Translation: And if he did not leave enough wealth, but left a child born during the kitabah, then he will work for his father's kitabah according to its installments. When he pays, we will rule that his father became free before his death, and the child will also be free, because the child is included in his kitabah, and the child's earnings are like his earnings, so he stands in his place in payment, and it becomes as if he left wealth. Commentary on his statement: 'And if he did not leave...' - And if the aforementioned mukatab did not leave property that could be sufficient for paying the kitabah payment, but he left a child who was born during the kitabah period, then that child will work for his father's kitabah according to its installments, and after payment, it will be ruled that his father became free before his death, and his child will also become free, because he is included in his kitabah.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Al-Hidayah. It addresses the scenario where a mukatab dies without sufficient property to pay off his kitabah, but leaves a child who was born during the period of the kitabah contract. In Hanafi law, such a child is automatically included in the father's kitabah contract because the child is considered part of the father's acquisition (kasb) during the kitabah period. Therefore, the child is obligated to work and earn to pay off the remaining installments of the father's kitabah. If the child succeeds in paying the entire amount, then retroactively the father is declared free from before his death, and the child also becomes free because he was included in the kitabah. The child's earnings are treated as the father's earnings, so it is as if the father left enough wealth after all. This ruling demonstrates the principle that a child born during kitabah is considered a dependent (tabi') of the father in the contract, and his labor is available to fulfill the contract's obligations.

Key Terms
سعایت (sa'ayah): To work and earn to pay off the kitabah installments; the child is required to work for the father's kitabah.
مولود في الكتابة (mawlud fi al-kitabah): A child born to the mukatab during the period when the kitabah contract was in effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, if a mukatab dies without enough property but leaves a child born during the kitabah, what happens?
A) The child is free immediately and not responsible for the kitabah
B) The child must work to pay off the father's kitabah installments, and upon payment both father and child become free
C) The child becomes a slave of the master until the father's kitabah is paid by someone else
D) The kitabah contract is void and the father remains a slave

Key Takeaway
A child born during kitabah is included in his father's kitabah and must work to pay off the installments; upon full payment, the father is retroactively freed and the child also becomes free.
